He took a rest and then\r\nswelled himself up and fetched a succession of admirable groans.\r\n\r\nSid snored on.\r\n\r\nTom was aggravated. He said, \xe2\x80\x9cSid, Sid!\xe2\x80\x9d and shook him. This course\r\nworked well, and Tom began to groan again. Sid yawned, stretched, then\r\nbrought himself up on his elbow with a snort, and began to stare at Tom.\r\nTom went on groaning. Sid said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom! Say, Tom!\xe2\x80\x9d [No response.] \xe2\x80\x9cHere, Tom! TOM! What is the matter,\r\nTom?\xe2\x80\x9d And he shook him and looked in his face anxiously.\r\n\r\nTom moaned out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t, Sid. Don\xe2\x80\x99t joggle me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, Tom? I must call auntie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo--never mind. It\xe2\x80\x99ll be over by and by, maybe. Don\xe2\x80\x99t call anybody.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I must! _Don\xe2\x80\x99t_ groan so, Tom, it\xe2\x80\x99s awful. How long you been this\r\nway?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHours. Ouch! Oh, don\xe2\x80\x99t stir so, Sid, you\xe2\x80\x99ll kill me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you wake me sooner? Oh, Tom, _don\xe2\x80\x99t!_ It makes my flesh\r\ncrawl to hear you. Tom, what is the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI forgive you everything, Sid. [Groan.] Everything you\xe2\x80\x99ve ever done to\r\nme. When I\xe2\x80\x99m gone--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Tom, you ain\xe2\x80\x99t dying, are you? Don\xe2\x80\x99t, Tom--oh, don\xe2\x80\x99t. Maybe--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI forgive everybody, Sid. [Groan.] Tell \xe2\x80\x99em so, Sid. And Sid, you give\r\nmy window-sash and my cat with one eye to that new girl that\xe2\x80\x99s come to\r\ntown, and tell her--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Sid had snatched his clothes and gone. Tom was suffering in reality,\r\nnow, so handsomely was his imagination working, and so his groans had\r\ngathered quite a genuine tone.\r\n\r\nSid flew downstairs and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Aunt Polly, come! Tom\xe2\x80\x99s dying!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDying!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x99m. Don\xe2\x80\x99t wait--come quick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRubbage! I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut she fled upstairs, nevertheless, with Sid and Mary at her heels.\r\nAnd her face grew white, too, and her lip trembled. When she reached the\r\nbedside she gasped out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, Tom! Tom, what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, auntie, I\xe2\x80\x99m--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with you--what is the matter with you, child?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, auntie, my sore toe\xe2\x80\x99s mortified!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old lady sank down into a chair and laughed a little, then cried a\r\nlittle, then did both together. This restored her and she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, what a turn you did give me. Now you shut up that nonsense and\r\nclimb out of this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe groans ceased and the pain vanished from the toe. The boy felt a\r\nlittle foolish, and he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt Polly, it _seemed_ mortified, and it hurt so I never minded my\r\ntooth at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour tooth, indeed! What\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with your tooth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of them\xe2\x80\x99s loose, and it aches perfectly awful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, there, now, don\xe2\x80\x99t begin that groaning again. Open your mouth.\r\nWell--your tooth _is_ loose, but you\xe2\x80\x99re not going to die about that.\r\nMary, get me a silk thread, and a chunk of fire out of the kitchen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, please, auntie, don\xe2\x80\x99t pull it out. It don\xe2\x80\x99t hurt any more. I wish\r\nI may never stir if it does. Please don\xe2\x80\x99t, auntie. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to stay\r\nhome from school.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you don\xe2\x80\x99t, don\xe2\x80\x99t you? So all this row was because you thought you\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nget to stay home from school and go a-fishing? Tom, Tom, I love you so,\r\nand you seem to try every way you can to break my old heart with your\r\noutrageousness.\xe2\x80\x9d By this time the dental instruments were ready. The old\r\nlady made one end of the silk thread fast to Tom\xe2\x80\x99s tooth with a loop\r\nand tied the other to the bedpost. Then she seized the chunk of fire and\r\nsuddenly thrust it almost into the boy\xe2\x80\x99s face. The tooth hung dangling\r\nby the bedpost, now.\r\n\r\nBut all trials bring their compensations. As Tom wended to school after\r\nbreakfast, he was the envy of every boy he met because the gap in his\r\nupper row of teeth enabled him to expectorate in a new and admirable\r\nway. He gathered quite a following of lads interested in the exhibition;\r\nand one that had cut his finger and had been a centre of fascination and\r\nhomage up to this time, now found himself suddenly without an adherent,\r\nand shorn of his glory. His heart was heavy, and he said with a disdain\r\nwhich he did not feel that it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t anything to spit like Tom Sawyer;\r\nbut another boy said, \xe2\x80\x9cSour grapes!\xe2\x80\x9d and he wandered away a dismantled\r\nhero.\r\n\r\nShortly Tom came upon the juvenile pariah of the village, Huckleberry\r\nFinn, son of the town drunkard. Huckleberry was cordially hated and\r\ndreaded by all the mothers of the town, because he was idle and lawless\r\nand vulgar and bad--and because all their children admired him so, and\r\ndelighted in his forbidden society, and wished they dared to be like\r\nhim. Tom was like the rest of the respectable boys, in that he envied\r\nHuckleberry his gaudy outcast condition, and was under strict orders\r\nnot to play with him. So he played with him every time he got a chance.\r\nHuckleberry was always dressed in the cast-off clothes of full-grown\r\nmen, and they were in perennial bloom and fluttering with rags. His hat\r\nwas a vast ruin with a wide crescent lopped out of its brim; his coat,\r\nwhen he wore one, hung nearly to his heels and had the rearward buttons\r\nfar down the back; but one suspender supported his trousers; the seat of\r\nthe trousers bagged low and contained nothing, the fringed legs dragged\r\nin the dirt when not rolled up.\r\n\r\nHuckleberry came and went, at his own free will. He slept on doorsteps\r\nin fine weather and in empty hogsheads in wet; he did not have to go to\r\nschool or to church, or call any being master or obey anybody; he could\r\ngo fishing or swimming when and where he chose, and stay as long as it\r\nsuited him; nobody forbade him to fight; he could sit up as late as he\r\npleased; he was always the first boy that went barefoot in the spring\r\nand the last to resume leather in the fall; he never had to wash, nor\r\nput on clean clothes; he could swear wonderfully. In a word, everything\r\nthat goes to make life precious that boy had. So thought every harassed,\r\nhampered, respectable boy in St. Petersburg.\r\n\r\nTom hailed the romantic outcast:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello, Huckleberry!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello yourself, and see how you like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that you got?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDead cat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLemme see him, Huck. My, he\xe2\x80\x99s pretty stiff. Where\xe2\x80\x99d you get him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBought him off\xe2\x80\x99n a boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you give?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI give a blue ticket and a bladder that I got at the slaughter-house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99d you get the blue ticket?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBought it off\xe2\x80\x99n Ben Rogers two weeks ago for a hoop-stick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay--what is dead cats good for, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood for? Cure warts with.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! Is that so? I know something that\xe2\x80\x99s better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bet you don\xe2\x80\x99t. What is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, spunk-water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpunk-water! I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t give a dern for spunk-water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you? D\xe2\x80\x99you ever try it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I hain\xe2\x80\x99t. But Bob Tanner did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho told you so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, he told Jeff Thatcher, and Jeff told Johnny Baker, and Johnny\r\ntold Jim Hollis, and Jim told Ben Rogers, and Ben told a nigger, and the\r\nnigger told me. There now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what of it? They\xe2\x80\x99ll all lie. Leastways all but the nigger. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know _him_. But I never see a nigger that _wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t_ lie. Shucks!\r\nNow you tell me how Bob Tanner done it, Huck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, he took and dipped his hand in a rotten stump where the rain-water\r\nwas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the daytime?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith his face to the stump?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Least I reckon so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he say anything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t reckon he did. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAha! Talk about trying to cure warts with spunk-water such a blame fool\r\nway as that! Why, that ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-going to do any good. You got to go all\r\nby yourself, to the middle of the woods, where you know there\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nspunk-water stump, and just as it\xe2\x80\x99s midnight you back up against the stump\r\nand jam your hand in and say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Barley-corn, barley-corn, injun-meal shorts, Spunk-water, spunk-water,\r\nswaller these warts,\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nand then walk away quick, eleven steps, with your eyes shut, and then\r\nturn around three times and walk home without speaking to anybody.\r\nBecause if you speak the charm\xe2\x80\x99s busted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that sounds like a good way; but that ain\xe2\x80\x99t the way Bob Tanner\r\ndone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, you can bet he didn\xe2\x80\x99t, becuz he\xe2\x80\x99s the wartiest boy in this\r\ntown; and he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have a wart on him if he\xe2\x80\x99d knowed how to work\r\nspunk-water. I\xe2\x80\x99ve took off thousands of warts off of my hands that way,\r\nHuck. I play with frogs so much that I\xe2\x80\x99ve always got considerable many\r\nwarts. Sometimes I take \xe2\x80\x99em off with a bean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, bean\xe2\x80\x99s good. I\xe2\x80\x99ve done that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you? What\xe2\x80\x99s your way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou take and split the bean, and cut the wart so as to get some blood,\r\nand then you put the blood on one piece of the bean and take and dig\r\na hole and bury it \xe2\x80\x99bout midnight at the crossroads in the dark of the\r\nmoon, and then you burn up the rest of the bean. You see that piece\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s got the blood on it will keep drawing and drawing, trying to\r\nfetch the other piece to it, and so that helps the blood to draw the\r\nwart, and pretty soon off she comes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s it, Huck--that\xe2\x80\x99s it; though when you\xe2\x80\x99re burying it if you\r\nsay \xe2\x80\x98Down bean; off wart; come no more to bother me!\xe2\x80\x99 it\xe2\x80\x99s better.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s the way Joe Harper does, and he\xe2\x80\x99s been nearly to Coonville and\r\nmost everywheres. But say--how do you cure \xe2\x80\x99em with dead cats?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you take your cat and go and get in the grave-yard \xe2\x80\x99long about\r\nmidnight when somebody that was wicked has been buried; and when it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmidnight a devil will come, or maybe two or three, but you can\xe2\x80\x99t see\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em, you can only hear something like the wind, or maybe hear \xe2\x80\x99em talk;\r\nand when they\xe2\x80\x99re taking that feller away, you heave your cat after \xe2\x80\x99em\r\nand say, \xe2\x80\x98Devil follow corpse, cat follow devil, warts follow cat, I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ndone with ye!\xe2\x80\x99 That\xe2\x80\x99ll fetch _any_ wart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSounds right. D\xe2\x80\x99you ever try it, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but old Mother Hopkins told me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I reckon it\xe2\x80\x99s so, then. Becuz they say she\xe2\x80\x99s a witch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay! Why, Tom, I _know_ she is. She witched pap. Pap says so his own\r\nself. He come along one day, and he see she was a-witching him, so he\r\ntook up a rock, and if she hadn\xe2\x80\x99t dodged, he\xe2\x80\x99d a got her. Well, that\r\nvery night he rolled off\xe2\x80\x99n a shed wher\xe2\x80\x99 he was a layin drunk, and broke\r\nhis arm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that\xe2\x80\x99s awful. How did he know she was a-witching him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord, pap can tell, easy. Pap says when they keep looking at you right\r\nstiddy, they\xe2\x80\x99re a-witching you. Specially if they mumble. Becuz when\r\nthey mumble they\xe2\x80\x99re saying the Lord\xe2\x80\x99s Prayer backards.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, Hucky, when you going to try the cat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-night. I reckon they\xe2\x80\x99ll come after old Hoss Williams to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut they buried him Saturday. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t they get him Saturday night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, how you talk! How could their charms work till midnight?--and\r\n_then_ it\xe2\x80\x99s Sunday. Devils don\xe2\x80\x99t slosh around much of a Sunday, I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nreckon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never thought of that. That\xe2\x80\x99s so. Lemme go with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course--if you ain\xe2\x80\x99t afeard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfeard! \xe2\x80\x99Tain\xe2\x80\x99t likely. Will you meow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes--and you meow back, if you get a chance. Last time, you kep\xe2\x80\x99 me\r\na-meowing around till old Hays went to throwing rocks at me and says\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Dern that cat!\xe2\x80\x99 and so I hove a brick through his window--but don\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\ntell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t meow that night, becuz auntie was watching me, but\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll meow this time. Say--what\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing but a tick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99d you get him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOut in the woods.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99ll you take for him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to sell him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. It\xe2\x80\x99s a mighty small tick, anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, anybody can run a tick down that don\xe2\x80\x99t belong to them. I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nsatisfied with it. It\xe2\x80\x99s a good enough tick for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSho, there\xe2\x80\x99s ticks a plenty. I could have a thousand of \xe2\x80\x99em if I wanted\r\nto.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, why don\xe2\x80\x99t you? Becuz you know mighty well you can\xe2\x80\x99t. This is a\r\npretty early tick, I reckon. It\xe2\x80\x99s the first one I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen this year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, Huck--I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you my tooth for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLess see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom got out a bit of paper and carefully unrolled it. Huckleberry viewed\r\nit wistfully. The temptation was very strong. At last he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it genuwyne?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom lifted his lip and showed the vacancy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, all right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Huckleberry, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s a trade.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom enclosed the tick in the percussion-cap box that had lately been the\r\npinchbug\xe2\x80\x99s prison, and the boys separated, each feeling wealthier than\r\nbefore.\r\n\r\nWhen Tom reached the little isolated frame school-house, he strode in\r\nbriskly, with the manner of one who had come with all honest speed. He\r\nhung his hat on a peg and flung himself into his seat with business-like\r\nalacrity. The master, throned on high in his great splint-bottom\r\narm-chair, was dozing, lulled by the drowsy hum of study. The\r\ninterruption roused him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThomas Sawyer!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom knew that when his name was pronounced in full, it meant trouble.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome up here. Now, sir, why are you late again, as usual?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom was about to take refuge in a lie, when he saw two long tails of\r\nyellow hair hanging down a back that he recognized by the electric\r\nsympathy of love; and by that form was _the only vacant place_ on the\r\ngirls\xe2\x80\x99 side of the school-house. He instantly said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I stopped to talk with Huckleberry Finn!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe master\xe2\x80\x99s pulse stood still, and he stared helplessly. The buzz of\r\nstudy ceased. The pupils wondered if this foolhardy boy had lost his\r\nmind. The master said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou--you did what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStopped to talk with Huckleberry Finn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no mistaking the words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThomas Sawyer, this is the most astounding confession I have ever\r\nlistened to. No mere ferule will answer for this offence. Take off your\r\njacket.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe master\xe2\x80\x99s arm performed until it was tired and the stock of switches\r\nnotably diminished. Then the order followed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, sir, go and sit with the girls! And let this be a warning to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe titter that rippled around the room appeared to abash the boy, but\r\nin reality that result was caused rather more by his worshipful awe\r\nof his unknown idol and the dread pleasure that lay in his high good\r\nfortune. He sat down upon the end of the pine bench and the girl hitched\r\nherself away from him with a toss of her head. Nudges and winks and\r\nwhispers traversed the room, but Tom sat still, with his arms upon the\r\nlong, low desk before him, and seemed to study his book.\r\n\r\nBy and by attention ceased from him, and the accustomed school murmur\r\nrose upon the dull air once more. Presently the boy began to steal\r\nfurtive glances at the girl. She observed it, \xe2\x80\x9cmade a mouth\xe2\x80\x9d at him\r\nand gave him the back of her head for the space of a minute. When she\r\ncautiously faced around again, a peach lay before her. She thrust it\r\naway. Tom gently put it back. She thrust it away again, but with less\r\nanimosity. Tom patiently returned it to its place. Then she let it\r\nremain. Tom scrawled on his slate, \xe2\x80\x9cPlease take it--I got more.\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\ngirl glanced at the words, but made no sign. Now the boy began to draw\r\nsomething on the slate, hiding his work with his left hand. For a time\r\nthe girl refused to notice; but her human curiosity presently began\r\nto manifest itself by hardly perceptible signs. The boy worked on,\r\napparently unconscious. The girl made a sort of non-committal attempt\r\nto see, but the boy did not betray that he was aware of it. At last she\r\ngave in and hesitatingly whispered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom partly uncovered a dismal caricature of a house with two gable ends\r\nto it and a corkscrew of smoke issuing from the chimney. Then the girl\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ninterest began to fasten itself upon the work and she forgot everything\r\nelse. When it was finished, she gazed a moment, then whispered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s nice--make a man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe artist erected a man in the front yard, that resembled a derrick. He\r\ncould have stepped over the house; but the girl was not hypercritical;\r\nshe was satisfied with the monster, and whispered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a beautiful man--now make me coming along.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom drew an hour-glass with a full moon and straw limbs to it and armed\r\nthe spreading fingers with a portentous fan. The girl said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s ever so nice--I wish I could draw.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s easy,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Tom, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll learn you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, will you? When?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt noon. Do you go home to dinner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll stay if you will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood--that\xe2\x80\x99s a whack. What\xe2\x80\x99s your name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecky Thatcher. What\xe2\x80\x99s yours? Oh, I know. It\xe2\x80\x99s Thomas Sawyer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the name they lick me by. I\xe2\x80\x99m Tom when I\xe2\x80\x99m good. You call me\r\nTom, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow Tom began to scrawl something on the slate, hiding the words from\r\nthe girl. But she was not backward this time. She begged to see. Tom\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo it ain\xe2\x80\x99t. You don\xe2\x80\x99t want to see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes I do, indeed I do. Please let me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll tell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo I won\xe2\x80\x99t--deed and deed and double deed won\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t tell anybody at all? Ever, as long as you live?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I won\xe2\x80\x99t ever tell _any_body. Now let me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _you_ don\xe2\x80\x99t want to see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow that you treat me so, I _will_ see.\xe2\x80\x9d And she put her small hand\r\nupon his and a little scuffle ensued, Tom pretending to resist in\r\nearnest but letting his hand slip by degrees till these words were\r\nrevealed: \xe2\x80\x9c_I love you_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you bad thing!\xe2\x80\x9d And she hit his hand a smart rap, but reddened and\r\nlooked pleased, nevertheless.\r\n\r\nJust at this juncture the boy felt a slow, fateful grip closing on his\r\near, and a steady lifting impulse. In that wise he was borne across the\r\nhouse and deposited in his own seat, under a peppering fire of giggles\r\nfrom the whole school. Then the master stood over him during a few awful\r\nmoments, and finally moved away to his throne without saying a word. But\r\nalthough Tom\xe2\x80\x99s ear tingled, his heart was jubilant.\r\n\r\nAs the school quieted down Tom made an honest effort to study, but\r\nthe turmoil within him was too great. In turn he took his place in the\r\nreading class and made a botch of it; then in the geography class and\r\nturned lakes into mountains, mountains into rivers, and rivers into\r\ncontinents, till chaos was come again; then in the spelling class, and\r\ngot \xe2\x80\x9cturned down,\xe2\x80\x9d by a succession of mere baby words, till he brought\r\nup at the foot and yielded up the pewter medal which he had worn with\r\nostentation for months.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII\r\n\r\nTHE harder Tom tried to fasten his mind on his book, the more his ideas\r\nwandered. So at last, with a sigh and a yawn, he gave it up. It seemed\r\nto him that the noon recess would never come. The air was utterly dead.\r\nThere was not a breath stirring. It was the sleepiest of sleepy days.\r\nThe drowsing murmur of the five and twenty studying scholars soothed\r\nthe soul like the spell that is in the murmur of bees. Away off in the\r\nflaming sunshine, Cardiff Hill lifted its soft green sides through a\r\nshimmering veil of heat, tinted with the purple of distance; a few birds\r\nfloated on lazy wing high in the air; no other living thing was visible\r\nbut some cows, and they were asleep. Tom\xe2\x80\x99s heart ached to be free, or\r\nelse to have something of interest to do to pass the dreary time.\r\nHis hand wandered into his pocket and his face lit up with a glow of\r\ngratitude that was prayer, though he did not know it. Then furtively\r\nthe percussion-cap box came out. He released the tick and put him on\r\nthe long flat desk. The creature probably glowed with a gratitude that\r\namounted to prayer, too, at this moment, but it was premature: for when\r\nhe started thankfully to travel off, Tom turned him aside with a pin and\r\nmade him take a new direction.\r\n\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s bosom friend sat next him, suffering just as Tom had been, and\r\nnow he was deeply and gratefully interested in this entertainment in\r\nan instant. This bosom friend was Joe Harper. The two boys were sworn\r\nfriends all the week, and embattled enemies on Saturdays. Joe took a\r\npin out of his lapel and began to assist in exercising the prisoner.\r\nThe sport grew in interest momently. Soon Tom said that they were\r\ninterfering with each other, and neither getting the fullest benefit\r\nof the tick. So he put Joe\xe2\x80\x99s slate on the desk and drew a line down the\r\nmiddle of it from top to bottom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cas long as he is on your side you can stir him up and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll let him alone; but if you let him get away and get on my side,\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re to leave him alone as long as I can keep him from crossing over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, go ahead; start him up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe tick escaped from Tom, presently, and crossed the equator. Joe\r\nharassed him awhile, and then he got away and crossed back again. This\r\nchange of base occurred often. While one boy was worrying the tick with\r\nabsorbing interest, the other would look on with interest as strong, the\r\ntwo heads bowed together over the slate, and the two souls dead to all\r\nthings else. At last luck seemed to settle and abide with Joe. The\r\ntick tried this, that, and the other course, and got as excited and as\r\nanxious as the boys themselves, but time and again just as he would\r\nhave victory in his very grasp, so to speak, and Tom\xe2\x80\x99s fingers would\r\nbe twitching to begin, Joe\xe2\x80\x99s pin would deftly head him off, and keep\r\npossession. At last Tom could stand it no longer. The temptation was too\r\nstrong. So he reached out and lent a hand with his pin. Joe was angry in\r\na moment. Said he:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, you let him alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI only just want to stir him up a little, Joe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t fair; you just let him alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlame it, I ain\xe2\x80\x99t going to stir him much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him alone, I tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shall--he\xe2\x80\x99s on my side of the line.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, Joe Harper, whose is that tick?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care whose tick he is--he\xe2\x80\x99s on my side of the line, and you\r\nsha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t touch him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll just bet I will, though. He\xe2\x80\x99s my tick and I\xe2\x80\x99ll do what I\r\nblame please with him, or die!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA tremendous whack came down on Tom\xe2\x80\x99s shoulders, and its duplicate on\r\nJoe\xe2\x80\x99s; and for the space of two minutes the dust continued to fly from\r\nthe two jackets and the whole school to enjoy it. The boys had been\r\ntoo absorbed to notice the hush that had stolen upon the school awhile\r\nbefore when the master came tiptoeing down the room and stood over them.\r\nHe had contemplated a good part of the performance before he contributed\r\nhis bit of variety to it.\r\n\r\nWhen school broke up at noon, Tom flew to Becky Thatcher, and whispered\r\nin her ear:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPut on your bonnet and let on you\xe2\x80\x99re going home; and when you get to\r\nthe corner, give the rest of \xe2\x80\x99em the slip, and turn down through the\r\nlane and come back. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go the other way and come it over \xe2\x80\x99em the same\r\nway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo the one went off with one group of scholars, and the other with\r\nanother. In a little while the two met at the bottom of the lane, and\r\nwhen they reached the school they had it all to themselves. Then they\r\nsat together, with a slate before them, and Tom gave Becky the pencil\r\nand held her hand in his, guiding it, and so created another surprising\r\nhouse. When the interest in art began to wane, the two fell to talking.\r\nTom was swimming in bliss. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you love rats?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! I hate them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I do, too--_live_ ones. But I mean dead ones, to swing round your\r\nhead with a string.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for rats much, anyway. What I like is chewing-gum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I should say so! I wish I had some now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you? I\xe2\x80\x99ve got some. I\xe2\x80\x99ll let you chew it awhile, but you must give\r\nit back to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was agreeable, so they chewed it turn about, and dangled their legs\r\nagainst the bench in excess of contentment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas you ever at a circus?\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and my pa\xe2\x80\x99s going to take me again some time, if I\xe2\x80\x99m good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI been to the circus three or four times--lots of times. Church ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nshucks to a circus. There\xe2\x80\x99s things going on at a circus all the time.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m going to be a clown in a circus when I grow up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, are you! That will be nice. They\xe2\x80\x99re so lovely, all spotted up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s so. And they get slathers of money--most a dollar a day, Ben\r\nRogers says. Say, Becky, was you ever engaged?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, engaged to be married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould you like to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon so. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. What is it like?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike? Why it ain\xe2\x80\x99t like anything. You only just tell a boy you won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\never have anybody but him, ever ever ever, and then you kiss and that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nall. Anybody can do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKiss? What do you kiss for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that, you know, is to--well, they always do that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverybody?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yes, everybody that\xe2\x80\x99s in love with each other. Do you remember\r\nwhat I wrote on the slate?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe--yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I tell _you_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe--yes--but some other time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not now--to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, _now_. Please, Becky--I\xe2\x80\x99ll whisper it, I\xe2\x80\x99ll whisper it ever so\r\neasy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBecky hesitating, Tom took silence for consent, and passed his arm about\r\nher waist and whispered the tale ever so softly, with his mouth close to\r\nher ear. And then he added:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you whisper it to me--just the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe resisted, for a while, and then said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou turn your face away so you can\xe2\x80\x99t see, and then I will. She bent timidly around till her breath stirred\r\nhis curls and whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cI--love--you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen she sprang away and ran around and around the desks and benches,\r\nwith Tom after her, and took refuge in a corner at last, with her little\r\nwhite apron to her face. Tom clasped her about her neck and pleaded:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Becky, it\xe2\x80\x99s all done--all over but the kiss. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you be afraid\r\nof that--it ain\xe2\x80\x99t anything at all. Please, Becky.\xe2\x80\x9d And he tugged at her\r\napron and the hands.\r\n\r\nBy and by she gave up, and let her hands drop; her face, all glowing\r\nwith the struggle, came up and submitted. Tom kissed the red lips and\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow it\xe2\x80\x99s all done, Becky. And always after this, you know, you ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\never to love anybody but me, and you ain\xe2\x80\x99t ever to marry anybody but me,\r\never never and forever. Will you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99ll never love anybody but you, Tom, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll never marry anybody\r\nbut you--and you ain\xe2\x80\x99t to ever marry anybody but me, either.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. Of course. That\xe2\x80\x99s _part_ of it. And always coming to school\r\nor when we\xe2\x80\x99re going home, you\xe2\x80\x99re to walk with me, when there ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nanybody looking--and you choose me and I choose you at parties, because\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s the way you do when you\xe2\x80\x99re engaged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s so nice. I never heard of it before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s ever so gay! Why, me and Amy Lawrence--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe big eyes told Tom his blunder and he stopped, confused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Tom! Then I ain\xe2\x80\x99t the first you\xe2\x80\x99ve ever been engaged to!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe child began to cry. Tom said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t cry, Becky, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for her any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you do, Tom--you know you do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom tried to put his arm about her neck, but she pushed him away and\r\nturned her face to the wall, and went on crying. Tom tried again, with\r\nsoothing words in his mouth, and was repulsed again. Then his pride was\r\nup, and he strode away and went outside. He stood about, restless and\r\nuneasy, for a while, glancing at the door, every now and then, hoping\r\nshe would repent and come to find him. But she did not. Then he began\r\nto feel badly and fear that he was in the wrong. It was a hard struggle\r\nwith him to make new advances, now, but he nerved himself to it and\r\nentered. She was still standing back there in the corner, sobbing, with\r\nher face to the wall. Tom\xe2\x80\x99s heart smote him. He went to her and stood a\r\nmoment, not knowing exactly how to proceed. Then he said hesitatingly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecky, I--I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for anybody but you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo reply--but sobs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecky\xe2\x80\x9d--pleadingly. \xe2\x80\x9cBecky, won\xe2\x80\x99t you say something?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMore sobs.\r\n\r\nTom got out his chiefest jewel, a brass knob from the top of an andiron,\r\nand passed it around her so that she could see it, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease, Becky, won\xe2\x80\x99t you take it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe struck it to the floor. Then Tom marched out of the house and over\r\nthe hills and far away, to return to school no more that day. Presently\r\nBecky began to suspect. She ran to the door; he was not in sight; she\r\nflew around to the play-yard; he was not there. Then she called:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom! Come back, Tom!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe listened intently, but there was no answer. She had no companions\r\nbut silence and loneliness. So she sat down to cry again and upbraid\r\nherself; and by this time the scholars began to gather again, and she\r\nhad to hide her griefs and still her broken heart and take up the cross\r\nof a long, dreary, aching afternoon, with none among the strangers about\r\nher to exchange sorrows with.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII\r\n\r\nTOM dodged hither and thither through lanes until he was well out of the\r\ntrack of returning scholars, and then fell into a moody jog. He crossed\r\na small \xe2\x80\x9cbranch\xe2\x80\x9d two or three times, because of a prevailing juvenile\r\nsuperstition that to cross water baffled pursuit. Half an hour later\r\nhe was disappearing behind the Douglas mansion on the summit of Cardiff\r\nHill, and the school-house was hardly distinguishable away off in the\r\nvalley behind him. He entered a dense wood, picked his pathless way to\r\nthe centre of it, and sat down on a mossy spot under a spreading oak.\r\nThere was not even a zephyr stirring; the dead noonday heat had even\r\nstilled the songs of the birds; nature lay in a trance that was broken\r\nby no sound but the occasional far-off hammering of a wood-pecker, and\r\nthis seemed to render the pervading silence and sense of loneliness the\r\nmore profound. The boy\xe2\x80\x99s soul was steeped in melancholy; his feelings\r\nwere in happy accord with his surroundings. He sat long with his elbows\r\non his knees and his chin in his hands, meditating. It seemed to him\r\nthat life was but a trouble, at best, and he more than half envied Jimmy\r\nHodges, so lately released; it must be very peaceful, he thought, to lie\r\nand slumber and dream forever and ever, with the wind whispering through\r\nthe trees and caressing the grass and the flowers over the grave, and\r\nnothing to bother and grieve about, ever any more. If he only had a\r\nclean Sunday-school record he could be willing to go, and be done with\r\nit all. Now as to this girl. What had he done? Nothing. He had meant\r\nthe best in the world, and been treated like a dog--like a very dog. She\r\nwould be sorry some day--maybe when it was too late. Ah, if he could only\r\ndie _temporarily_!\r\n\r\nBut the elastic heart of youth cannot be compressed into one constrained\r\nshape long at a time. Tom presently began to drift insensibly back into\r\nthe concerns of this life again. What if he turned his back, now, and\r\ndisappeared mysteriously? What if he went away--ever so far away, into\r\nunknown countries beyond the seas--and never came back any more! How\r\nwould she feel then! The idea of being a clown recurred to him now, only\r\nto fill him with disgust. For frivolity and jokes and spotted tights\r\nwere an offense, when they intruded themselves upon a spirit that was\r\nexalted into the vague august realm of the romantic. No, he would be\r\na soldier, and return after long years, all war-worn and illustrious.\r\nNo--better still, he would join the Indians, and hunt buffaloes and go on\r\nthe warpath in the mountain ranges and the trackless great plains of the\r\nFar West, and away in the future come back a great chief, bristling with\r\nfeathers, hideous with paint, and prance into Sunday-school, some drowsy\r\nsummer morning, with a blood-curdling war-whoop, and sear the eyeballs\r\nof all his companions with unappeasable envy. But no, there was\r\nsomething gaudier even than this. He would be a pirate! That was it!\r\n_now_ his future lay plain before him, and glowing with unimaginable\r\nsplendor. How his name would fill the world, and make people shudder!\r\nHow gloriously he would go plowing the dancing seas, in his long, low,\r\nblack-hulled racer, the Spirit of the Storm, with his grisly flag flying\r\nat the fore! And at the zenith of his fame, how he would suddenly appear\r\nat the old village and stalk into church, brown and weather-beaten, in\r\nhis black velvet doublet and trunks, his great jack-boots, his crimson\r\nsash, his belt bristling with horse-pistols, his crime-rusted cutlass\r\nat his side, his slouch hat with waving plumes, his black flag unfurled,\r\nwith the skull and crossbones on it, and hear with swelling ecstasy\r\nthe whisperings, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s Tom Sawyer the Pirate!--the Black Avenger of the\r\nSpanish Main!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYes, it was settled; his career was determined. He would run away from\r\nhome and enter upon it. He would start the very next morning. Therefore\r\nhe must now begin to get ready. He would collect his resources together.\r\nHe went to a rotten log near at hand and began to dig under one end of\r\nit with his Barlow knife. He soon struck wood that sounded hollow. He\r\nput his hand there and uttered this incantation impressively:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat hasn\xe2\x80\x99t come here, come! What\xe2\x80\x99s here, stay here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he scraped away the dirt, and exposed a pine shingle. He took it\r\nup and disclosed a shapely little treasure-house whose bottom and sides\r\nwere of shingles. In it lay a marble. Tom\xe2\x80\x99s astonishment was bound-less!\r\nHe scratched his head with a perplexed air, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that beats anything!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he tossed the marble away pettishly, and stood cogitating. The\r\ntruth was, that a superstition of his had failed, here, which he and\r\nall his comrades had always looked upon as infallible. If you buried\r\na marble with certain necessary incantations, and left it alone a\r\nfortnight, and then opened the place with the incantation he had just\r\nused, you would find that all the marbles you had ever lost had gathered\r\nthemselves together there, meantime, no matter how widely they had been\r\nseparated. But now, this thing had actually and unquestionably failed.\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s whole structure of faith was shaken to its foundations. He had\r\nmany a time heard of this thing succeeding but never of its failing\r\nbefore. It did not occur to him that he had tried it several times\r\nbefore, himself, but could never find the hiding-places afterward. He\r\npuzzled over the matter some time, and finally decided that some witch\r\nhad interfered and broken the charm. He thought he would satisfy himself\r\non that point; so he searched around till he found a small sandy spot\r\nwith a little funnel-shaped depression in it. He laid himself down and\r\nput his mouth close to this depression and called--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoodle-bug, doodle-bug, tell me what I want to know! Doodle-bug,\r\ndoodle-bug, tell me what I want to know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sand began to work, and presently a small black bug appeared for a\r\nsecond and then darted under again in a fright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe dasn\xe2\x80\x99t tell! So it _was_ a witch that done it. I just knowed it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe well knew the futility of trying to contend against witches, so he\r\ngave up discouraged. But it occurred to him that he might as well have\r\nthe marble he had just thrown away, and therefore he went and made a\r\npatient search for it. But he could not find it. Now he went back to his\r\ntreasure-house and carefully placed himself just as he had been standing\r\nwhen he tossed the marble away; then he took another marble from his\r\npocket and tossed it in the same way, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrother, go find your brother!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe watched where it stopped, and went there and looked. But it must\r\nhave fallen short or gone too far; so he tried twice more. The last\r\nrepetition was successful. The two marbles lay within a foot of each\r\nother.\r\n\r\nJust here the blast of a toy tin trumpet came faintly down the green\r\naisles of the forest. Tom flung off his jacket and trousers, turned\r\na suspender into a belt, raked away some brush behind the rotten log,\r\ndisclosing a rude bow and arrow, a lath sword and a tin trumpet, and\r\nin a moment had seized these things and bounded away, barelegged,\r\nwith fluttering shirt. He presently halted under a great elm, blew an\r\nanswering blast, and then began to tiptoe and look warily out, this way\r\nand that. He said cautiously--to an imaginary company:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold, my merry men! Keep hid till I blow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow appeared Joe Harper, as airily clad and elaborately armed as Tom.\r\nTom called:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold! Who comes here into Sherwood Forest without my pass?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGuy of Guisborne wants no man\xe2\x80\x99s pass. Who art thou that--that--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDares to hold such language,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom, prompting--for they talked \xe2\x80\x9cby\r\nthe book,\xe2\x80\x9d from memory.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho art thou that dares to hold such language?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI, indeed! I am Robin Hood, as thy caitiff carcase soon shall know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen art thou indeed that famous outlaw? Right gladly will I dispute\r\nwith thee the passes of the merry wood. Have at thee!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey took their lath swords, dumped their other traps on the ground,\r\nstruck a fencing attitude, foot to foot, and began a grave, careful\r\ncombat, \xe2\x80\x9ctwo up and two down.\xe2\x80\x9d Presently Tom said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, if you\xe2\x80\x99ve got the hang, go it lively!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo they \xe2\x80\x9cwent it lively,\xe2\x80\x9d panting and perspiring with the work. By and\r\nby Tom shouted:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFall! fall! Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you fall?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t! Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you fall yourself? You\xe2\x80\x99re getting the worst of\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that ain\xe2\x80\x99t anything. I can\xe2\x80\x99t fall; that ain\xe2\x80\x99t the way it is in the\r\nbook. The book says, \xe2\x80\x98Then with one back-handed stroke he slew poor Guy\r\nof Guisborne.\xe2\x80\x99 You\xe2\x80\x99re to turn around and let me hit you in the back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no getting around the authorities, so Joe turned, received the\r\nwhack and fell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Joe, getting up, \xe2\x80\x9cyou got to let me kill _you_. That\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I can\xe2\x80\x99t do that, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t in the book.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s blamed mean--that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, say, Joe, you can be Friar Tuck or Much the miller\xe2\x80\x99s son, and lam\r\nme with a quarter-staff; or I\xe2\x80\x99ll be the Sheriff of Nottingham and you be\r\nRobin Hood a little while and kill me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was satisfactory, and so these adventures were carried out. Then\r\nTom became Robin Hood again, and was allowed by the treacherous nun to\r\nbleed his strength away through his neglected wound. And at last Joe,\r\nrepresenting a whole tribe of weeping outlaws, dragged him sadly forth,\r\ngave his bow into his feeble hands, and Tom said, \xe2\x80\x9cWhere this arrow\r\nfalls, there bury poor Robin Hood under the greenwood tree.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he\r\nshot the arrow and fell back and would have died, but he lit on a nettle\r\nand sprang up too gaily for a corpse.\r\n\r\nThe boys dressed themselves, hid their accoutrements, and went off\r\ngrieving that there were no outlaws any more, and wondering what modern\r\ncivilization could claim to have done to compensate for their loss.\r\nThey said they would rather be outlaws a year in Sherwood Forest than\r\nPresident of the United States forever.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX\r\n\r\nAT half-past nine, that night, Tom and Sid were sent to bed, as usual.\r\nThey said their prayers, and Sid was soon asleep. Tom lay awake and\r\nwaited, in restless impatience. When it seemed to him that it must be\r\nnearly daylight, he heard the clock strike ten! This was despair. He\r\nwould have tossed and fidgeted, as his nerves demanded, but he was\r\nafraid he might wake Sid. So he lay still, and stared up into the dark.\r\nEverything was dismally still. By and by, out of the stillness, little,\r\nscarcely perceptible noises began to emphasize themselves. The ticking\r\nof the clock began to bring itself into notice. Old beams began to crack\r\nmysteriously. The stairs creaked faintly. Evidently spirits were abroad.\r\nA measured, muffled snore issued from Aunt Polly\xe2\x80\x99s chamber. And now the\r\ntiresome chirping of a cricket that no human ingenuity could locate,\r\nbegan. Next the ghastly ticking of a death-watch in the wall at the\r\nbed\xe2\x80\x99s head made Tom shudder--it meant that somebody\xe2\x80\x99s days were numbered.\r\nThen the howl of a far-off dog rose on the night air, and was answered\r\nby a fainter howl from a remoter distance. Tom was in an agony. At last\r\nhe was satisfied that time had ceased and eternity begun; he began to\r\ndoze, in spite of himself; the clock chimed eleven, but he did not hear\r\nit. And then there came, mingling with his half-formed dreams, a most\r\nmelancholy caterwauling. The raising of a neighboring window disturbed\r\nhim. A cry of \xe2\x80\x9cScat! you devil!\xe2\x80\x9d and the crash of an empty bottle\r\nagainst the back of his aunt\xe2\x80\x99s woodshed brought him wide awake, and a\r\nsingle minute later he was dressed and out of the window and creeping\r\nalong the roof of the \xe2\x80\x9cell\xe2\x80\x9d on all fours. He \xe2\x80\x9cmeow\xe2\x80\x99d\xe2\x80\x9d with caution once\r\nor twice, as he went; then jumped to the roof of the woodshed and thence\r\nto the ground. Huckleberry Finn was there, with his dead cat. The boys\r\nmoved off and disappeared in the gloom. At the end of half an hour they\r\nwere wading through the tall grass of the graveyard.\r\n\r\nIt was a graveyard of the old-fashioned Western kind. It was on a hill,\r\nabout a mile and a half from the village. It had a crazy board fence\r\naround it, which leaned inward in places, and outward the rest of the\r\ntime, but stood upright nowhere. Grass and weeds grew rank over the\r\nwhole cemetery. All the old graves were sunken in, there was not a\r\ntombstone on the place; round-topped, worm-eaten boards staggered over\r\nthe graves, leaning for support and finding none. \xe2\x80\x9cSacred to the memory\r\nof\xe2\x80\x9d So-and-So had been painted on them once, but it could no longer have\r\nbeen read, on the most of them, now, even if there had been light.\r\n\r\nA faint wind moaned through the trees, and Tom feared it might be the\r\nspirits of the dead, complaining at being disturbed. The boys talked\r\nlittle, and only under their breath, for the time and the place and the\r\npervading solemnity and silence oppressed their spirits. They found the\r\nsharp new heap they were seeking, and ensconced themselves within the\r\nprotection of three great elms that grew in a bunch within a few feet of\r\nthe grave.\r\n\r\nThen they waited in silence for what seemed a long time. The hooting of\r\na distant owl was all the sound that troubled the dead stillness. Tom\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreflections grew oppressive. He must force some talk. So he said in a\r\nwhisper:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHucky, do you believe the dead people like it for us to be here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuckleberry whispered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wisht I knowed. It\xe2\x80\x99s awful solemn like, _ain\xe2\x80\x99t_ it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bet it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a considerable pause, while the boys canvassed this matter\r\ninwardly. Then Tom whispered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, Hucky--do you reckon Hoss Williams hears us talking?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO\xe2\x80\x99 course he does. Least his sperrit does.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom, after a pause:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I\xe2\x80\x99d said Mister Williams. But I never meant any harm. Everybody\r\ncalls him Hoss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA body can\xe2\x80\x99t be too partic\xe2\x80\x99lar how they talk \xe2\x80\x99bout these-yer dead\r\npeople, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was a damper, and conversation died again.\r\n\r\nPresently Tom seized his comrade\xe2\x80\x99s arm and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d And the two clung together with beating hearts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSh! There \xe2\x80\x99tis again! Didn\xe2\x80\x99t you hear it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere! Now you hear it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord, Tom, they\xe2\x80\x99re coming! They\xe2\x80\x99re coming, sure. What\xe2\x80\x99ll we do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dono. Think they\xe2\x80\x99ll see us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Tom, they can see in the dark, same as cats. I wisht I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t be afeard. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe they\xe2\x80\x99ll bother us. We ain\xe2\x80\x99t doing\r\nany harm. If we keep perfectly still, maybe they won\xe2\x80\x99t notice us at\r\nall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll try to, Tom, but, Lord, I\xe2\x80\x99m all of a shiver.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boys bent their heads together and scarcely breathed. A muffled\r\nsound of voices floated up from the far end of the graveyard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook! See there!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Tom. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s devil-fire. Oh, Tom, this is awful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSome vague figures approached through the gloom, swinging an\r\nold-fashioned tin lantern that freckled the ground with innumerable\r\nlittle spangles of light. Presently Huckleberry whispered with a\r\nshudder:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the devils sure enough. Three of \xe2\x80\x99em! Lordy, Tom, we\xe2\x80\x99re goners!\r\nCan you pray?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll try, but don\xe2\x80\x99t you be afeard. They ain\xe2\x80\x99t going to hurt us. \xe2\x80\x98Now I\r\nlay me down to sleep, I--\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re _humans_! One of \xe2\x80\x99em is, anyway. One of \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x99s old Muff Potter\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nvoice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo--\xe2\x80\x99tain\xe2\x80\x99t so, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bet I know it. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you stir nor budge. He ain\xe2\x80\x99t sharp enough to\r\nnotice us. Drunk, the same as usual, likely--blamed old rip!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, I\xe2\x80\x99ll keep still. Now they\xe2\x80\x99re stuck. Can\xe2\x80\x99t find it. Here they\r\ncome again. Now they\xe2\x80\x99re hot. Cold again. Hot again. Red hot! They\xe2\x80\x99re\r\np\xe2\x80\x99inted right, this time. Say, Huck, I know another o\xe2\x80\x99 them voices; it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nInjun Joe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s so--that murderin\xe2\x80\x99 half-breed! I\xe2\x80\x99d druther they was devils a dern\r\nsight. What kin they be up to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe whisper died wholly out, now, for the three men had reached the\r\ngrave and stood within a few feet of the boys\xe2\x80\x99 hiding-place.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere it is,\xe2\x80\x9d said the third voice; and the owner of it held the lantern\r\nup and revealed the face of young Doctor Robinson.\r\n\r\nPotter and Injun Joe were carrying a handbarrow with a rope and a couple\r\nof shovels on it. They cast down their load and began to open the grave.\r\nThe doctor put the lantern at the head of the grave and came and sat\r\ndown with his back against one of the elm trees. He was so close the\r\nboys could have touched him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurry, men!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in a low voice; \xe2\x80\x9cthe moon might come out at any\r\nmoment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey growled a response and went on digging. For some time there was no\r\nnoise but the grating sound of the spades discharging their freight of\r\nmould and gravel. It was very monotonous. Finally a spade struck upon\r\nthe coffin with a dull woody accent, and within another minute or two\r\nthe men had hoisted it out on the ground. They pried off the lid with\r\ntheir shovels, got out the body and dumped it rudely on the ground. The\r\nmoon drifted from behind the clouds and exposed the pallid face.\r\nThe barrow was got ready and the corpse placed on it, covered with a\r\nblanket, and bound to its place with the rope. Potter took out a large\r\nspring-knife and cut off the dangling end of the rope and then said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow the cussed thing\xe2\x80\x99s ready, Sawbones, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll just out with\r\nanother five, or here she stays.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the talk!\xe2\x80\x9d said Injun Joe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, what does this mean?\xe2\x80\x9d said the doctor. \xe2\x80\x9cYou required your\r\npay in advance, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve paid you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and you done more than that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Injun Joe, approaching the\r\ndoctor, who was now standing. \xe2\x80\x9cFive years ago you drove me away from\r\nyour father\xe2\x80\x99s kitchen one night, when I come to ask for something to\r\neat, and you said I warn\xe2\x80\x99t there for any good; and when I swore I\xe2\x80\x99d get\r\neven with you if it took a hundred years, your father had me jailed for\r\na vagrant. Did you think I\xe2\x80\x99d forget? The Injun blood ain\xe2\x80\x99t in me for\r\nnothing. And now I\xe2\x80\x99ve _got_ you, and you got to _settle_, you know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was threatening the doctor, with his fist in his face, by this time.\r\nThe doctor struck out suddenly and stretched the ruffian on the ground.\r\nPotter dropped his knife, and exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, now, don\xe2\x80\x99t you hit my pard!\xe2\x80\x9d and the next moment he had grappled\r\nwith the doctor and the two were struggling with might and main,\r\ntrampling the grass and tearing the ground with their heels. Injun Joe\r\nsprang to his feet, his eyes flaming with passion, snatched up Potter\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nknife, and went creeping, catlike and stooping, round and round about\r\nthe combatants, seeking an opportunity. All at once the doctor flung\r\nhimself free, seized the heavy headboard of Williams\xe2\x80\x99 grave and felled\r\nPotter to the earth with it--and in the same instant the half-breed saw\r\nhis chance and drove the knife to the hilt in the young man\xe2\x80\x99s breast. He\r\nreeled and fell partly upon Potter, flooding him with his blood, and in\r\nthe same moment the clouds blotted out the dreadful spectacle and the\r\ntwo frightened boys went speeding away in the dark.\r\n\r\nPresently, when the moon emerged again, Injun Joe was standing over the\r\ntwo forms, contemplating them. The doctor murmured inarticulately, gave\r\na long gasp or two and was still. The half-breed muttered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_That_ score is settled--damn you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he robbed the body. After which he put the fatal knife in Potter\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nopen right hand, and sat down on the dismantled coffin. Three--four--five\r\nminutes passed, and then Potter began to stir and moan. His hand closed\r\nupon the knife; he raised it, glanced at it, and let it fall, with a\r\nshudder. Then he sat up, pushing the body from him, and gazed at it, and\r\nthen around him, confusedly. His eyes met Joe\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord, how is this, Joe?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a dirty business,\xe2\x80\x9d said Joe, without moving.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you do it for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI! I never done it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here! That kind of talk won\xe2\x80\x99t wash.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPotter trembled and grew white.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought I\xe2\x80\x99d got sober. I\xe2\x80\x99d no business to drink to-night. But it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nin my head yet--worse\xe2\x80\x99n when we started here. I\xe2\x80\x99m all in a muddle;\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t recollect anything of it, hardly. Tell me, Joe--_honest_, now,\r\nold feller--did I do it? Joe, I never meant to--\xe2\x80\x99pon my soul and honor, I\r\nnever meant to, Joe. Tell me how it was, Joe. Oh, it\xe2\x80\x99s awful--and him so\r\nyoung and promising.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you two was scuffling, and he fetched you one with the headboard\r\nand you fell flat; and then up you come, all reeling and staggering\r\nlike, and snatched the knife and jammed it into him, just as he fetched\r\nyou another awful clip--and here you\xe2\x80\x99ve laid, as dead as a wedge til\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what I was a-doing. I wish I may die this minute if I\r\ndid. It was all on account of the whiskey and the excitement, I reckon.\r\nI never used a weepon in my life before, Joe. I\xe2\x80\x99ve fought, but never\r\nwith weepons. They\xe2\x80\x99ll all say that. Joe, don\xe2\x80\x99t tell! Say you won\xe2\x80\x99t tell,\r\nJoe--that\xe2\x80\x99s a good feller. I always liked you, Joe, and stood up for you,\r\ntoo. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you remember? You _won\xe2\x80\x99t_ tell, _will_ you, Joe?\xe2\x80\x9d And the\r\npoor creature dropped on his knees before the stolid murderer, and\r\nclasped his appealing hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you\xe2\x80\x99ve always been fair and square with me, Muff Potter, and I\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t go back on you. There, now, that\xe2\x80\x99s as fair as a man can say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Joe, you\xe2\x80\x99re an angel. I\xe2\x80\x99ll bless you for this the longest day I\r\nlive.\xe2\x80\x9d And Potter began to cry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, now, that\xe2\x80\x99s enough of that. This ain\xe2\x80\x99t any time for blubbering.\r\nYou be off yonder way and I\xe2\x80\x99ll go this. Move, now, and don\xe2\x80\x99t leave any\r\ntracks behind you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPotter started on a trot that quickly increased to a run. The half-breed\r\nstood looking after him. He muttered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf he\xe2\x80\x99s as much stunned with the lick and fuddled with the rum as he\r\nhad the look of being, he won\xe2\x80\x99t think of the knife till he\xe2\x80\x99s gone so\r\nfar he\xe2\x80\x99ll be afraid to come back after it to such a place by\r\nhimself--chicken-heart!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTwo or three minutes later the murdered man, the blanketed corpse, the\r\nlidless coffin, and the open grave were under no inspection but the\r\nmoon\xe2\x80\x99s. The stillness was complete again, too.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X\r\n\r\nTHE two boys flew on and on, toward the village, speechless with\r\nhorror. They glanced backward over their shoulders from time to time,\r\napprehensively, as if they feared they might be followed. Every stump\r\nthat started up in their path seemed a man and an enemy, and made them\r\ncatch their breath; and as they sped by some outlying cottages that lay\r\nnear the village, the barking of the aroused watch-dogs seemed to give\r\nwings to their feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf we can only get to the old tannery before we break down!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered\r\nTom, in short catches between breaths. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t stand it much longer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuckleberry\xe2\x80\x99s hard pantings were his only reply, and the boys fixed\r\ntheir eyes on the goal of their hopes and bent to their work to win it.\r\nThey gained steadily on it, and at last, breast to breast, they burst\r\nthrough the open door and fell grateful and exhausted in the sheltering\r\nshadows beyond. By and by their pulses slowed down, and Tom whispered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuckleberry, what do you reckon\xe2\x80\x99ll come of this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf Doctor Robinson dies, I reckon hanging\xe2\x80\x99ll come of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you though?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I _know_ it, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom thought a while, then he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99ll tell? We?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you talking about? S\xe2\x80\x99pose something happened and Injun Joe\r\n_didn\xe2\x80\x99t_ hang? Why, he\xe2\x80\x99d kill us some time or other, just as dead sure\r\nas we\xe2\x80\x99re a laying here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s just what I was thinking to myself, Huck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf anybody tells, let Muff Potter do it, if he\xe2\x80\x99s fool enough. He\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngenerally drunk enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom said nothing--went on thinking. Presently he whispered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck, Muff Potter don\xe2\x80\x99t know it. How can he tell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the reason he don\xe2\x80\x99t know it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause he\xe2\x80\x99d just got that whack when Injun Joe done it. D\xe2\x80\x99you reckon\r\nhe could see anything? D\xe2\x80\x99you reckon he knowed anything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy hokey, that\xe2\x80\x99s so, Tom!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd besides, look-a-here--maybe that whack done for _him_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, \xe2\x80\x99taint likely, Tom. He had liquor in him; I could see that; and\r\nbesides, he always has. Well, when pap\xe2\x80\x99s full, you might take and belt\r\nhim over the head with a church and you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t phase him. He says so,\r\nhis own self. So it\xe2\x80\x99s the same with Muff Potter, of course. But if a man\r\nwas dead sober, I reckon maybe that whack might fetch him; I dono.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter another reflective silence, Tom said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHucky, you sure you can keep mum?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, we _got_ to keep mum. You know that. That Injun devil wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmake any more of drownding us than a couple of cats, if we was to squeak\r\n\xe2\x80\x99bout this and they didn\xe2\x80\x99t hang him. Now, look-a-here, Tom, less take\r\nand swear to one another--that\xe2\x80\x99s what we got to do--swear to keep mum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m agreed. It\xe2\x80\x99s the best thing. Would you just hold hands and swear\r\nthat we--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, that wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do for this. That\xe2\x80\x99s good enough for little\r\nrubbishy common things--specially with gals, cuz _they_ go back on you\r\nanyway, and blab if they get in a huff--but there orter be writing \xe2\x80\x99bout\r\na big thing like this. And blood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s whole being applauded this idea. It was deep, and dark, and awful;\r\nthe hour, the circumstances, the surroundings, were in keeping with it.\r\nHe picked up a clean pine shingle that lay in the moon-light, took a\r\nlittle fragment of \xe2\x80\x9cred keel\xe2\x80\x9d out of his pocket, got the moon on\r\nhis work, and painfully scrawled these lines, emphasizing each slow\r\ndown-stroke by clamping his tongue between his teeth, and letting up the\r\npressure on the up-strokes. [See next page.]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck Finn and Tom Sawyer swears they will keep mum about This and They\r\nwish They may Drop down dead in Their Tracks if They ever Tell and Rot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuckleberry was filled with admiration of Tom\xe2\x80\x99s facility in writing, and\r\nthe sublimity of his language. He at once took a pin from his lapel and\r\nwas going to prick his flesh, but Tom said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold on! Don\xe2\x80\x99t do that. A pin\xe2\x80\x99s brass. It might have verdigrease on\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s verdigrease?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s p\xe2\x80\x99ison. That\xe2\x80\x99s what it is. You just swaller some of it once--you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nsee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Tom unwound the thread from one of his needles, and each boy pricked\r\nthe ball of his thumb and squeezed out a drop of blood. In time, after\r\nmany squeezes, Tom managed to sign his initials, using the ball of his\r\nlittle finger for a pen. Then he showed Huckleberry how to make an H and\r\nan F, and the oath was complete. They buried the shingle close to the\r\nwall, with some dismal ceremonies and incantations, and the fetters\r\nthat bound their tongues were considered to be locked and the key thrown\r\naway.\r\n\r\nA figure crept stealthily through a break in the other end of the ruined\r\nbuilding, now, but they did not notice it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Huckleberry, \xe2\x80\x9cdoes this keep us from _ever_\r\ntelling--_always_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course it does. It don\xe2\x80\x99t make any difference _what_ happens, we got\r\nto keep mum. We\xe2\x80\x99d drop down dead--don\xe2\x80\x99t _you_ know that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I reckon that\xe2\x80\x99s so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey continued to whisper for some little time. Presently a dog set up\r\na long, lugubrious howl just outside--within ten feet of them. The boys\r\nclasped each other suddenly, in an agony of fright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich of us does he mean?\xe2\x80\x9d gasped Huckleberry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dono--peep through the crack. Quick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, _you_, Tom!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t--I can\xe2\x80\x99t _do_ it, Huck!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease, Tom. There \xe2\x80\x99tis again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, lordy, I\xe2\x80\x99m thankful!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Tom. \xe2\x80\x9cI know his voice. It\xe2\x80\x99s Bull\r\nHarbison.\xe2\x80\x9d *\r\n\r\n[* If Mr. Harbison owned a slave named Bull, Tom would have spoken of\r\nhim as \xe2\x80\x9cHarbison\xe2\x80\x99s Bull,\xe2\x80\x9d but a son or a dog of that name was \xe2\x80\x9cBull\r\nHarbison.\xe2\x80\x9d]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s good--I tell you, Tom, I was most scared to death; I\xe2\x80\x99d a bet\r\nanything it was a _stray_ dog.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe dog howled again. The boys\xe2\x80\x99 hearts sank once more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my! that ain\xe2\x80\x99t no Bull Harbison!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Huckleberry. \xe2\x80\x9c_Do_,\r\nTom!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom, quaking with fear, yielded, and put his eye to the crack. His\r\nwhisper was hardly audible when he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Huck, _its a stray dog_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuick, Tom, quick! Who does he mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck, he must mean us both--we\xe2\x80\x99re right together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Tom, I reckon we\xe2\x80\x99re goners. I reckon there ain\xe2\x80\x99t no mistake \xe2\x80\x99bout\r\nwhere _I\xe2\x80\x99ll_ go to. I been so wicked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDad fetch it! This comes of playing hookey and doing everything a\r\nfeller\xe2\x80\x99s told _not_ to do. I might a been good, like Sid, if I\xe2\x80\x99d a\r\ntried--but no, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, of course. But if ever I get off this time,\r\nI lay I\xe2\x80\x99ll just _waller_ in Sunday-schools!\xe2\x80\x9d And Tom began to snuffle a\r\nlittle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_You_ bad!\xe2\x80\x9d and Huckleberry began to snuffle too. \xe2\x80\x9cConsound it, Tom\r\nSawyer, you\xe2\x80\x99re just old pie, \xe2\x80\x99long-side o\xe2\x80\x99 what I am. Oh, _lordy_,\r\nlordy, lordy, I wisht I only had half your chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom choked off and whispered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook, Hucky, look! He\xe2\x80\x99s got his _back_ to us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHucky looked, with joy in his heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he has, by jingoes! Did he before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he did. But I, like a fool, never thought. Oh, this is bully, you\r\nknow. _Now_ who can he mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe howling stopped. Tom pricked up his ears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSh! What\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSounds like--like hogs grunting. No--it\xe2\x80\x99s somebody snoring, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat _is_ it! Where \xe2\x80\x99bouts is it, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bleeve it\xe2\x80\x99s down at \xe2\x80\x99tother end. Sounds so, anyway. Pap used to sleep\r\nthere, sometimes, \xe2\x80\x99long with the hogs, but laws bless you, he just lifts\r\nthings when _he_ snores. Besides, I reckon he ain\xe2\x80\x99t ever coming back to\r\nthis town any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe spirit of adventure rose in the boys\xe2\x80\x99 souls once more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHucky, do you das\xe2\x80\x99t to go if I lead?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like to, much. Tom, s\xe2\x80\x99pose it\xe2\x80\x99s Injun Joe!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom quailed. But presently the temptation rose up strong again and the\r\nboys agreed to try, with the understanding that they would take to their\r\nheels if the snoring stopped. So they went tiptoeing stealthily down,\r\nthe one behind the other. When they had got to within five steps of the\r\nsnorer, Tom stepped on a stick, and it broke with a sharp snap. The man\r\nmoaned, writhed a little, and his face came into the moonlight. It was\r\nMuff Potter. The boys\xe2\x80\x99 hearts had stood still, and their hopes too,\r\nwhen the man moved, but their fears passed away now. They tip-toed out,\r\nthrough the broken weather-boarding, and stopped at a little distance\r\nto exchange a parting word. That long, lugubrious howl rose on the night\r\nair again! They turned and saw the strange dog standing within a few\r\nfeet of where Potter was lying, and _facing_ Potter, with his nose\r\npointing heavenward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, geeminy, it\xe2\x80\x99s _him_!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed both boys, in a breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, Tom--they say a stray dog come howling around Johnny Miller\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse, \xe2\x80\x99bout midnight, as much as two weeks ago; and a whippoorwill come\r\nin and lit on the banisters and sung, the very same evening; and there\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t anybody dead there yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I know that. And suppose there ain\xe2\x80\x99t. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t Gracie Miller fall\r\nin the kitchen fire and burn herself terrible the very next Saturday?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but she ain\xe2\x80\x99t _dead_. And what\xe2\x80\x99s more, she\xe2\x80\x99s getting better, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, you wait and see. She\xe2\x80\x99s a goner, just as dead sure as Muff\r\nPotter\xe2\x80\x99s a goner. That\xe2\x80\x99s what the niggers say, and they know all about\r\nthese kind of things, Huck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen they separated, cogitating. When Tom crept in at his bedroom window\r\nthe night was almost spent. He undressed with excessive caution, and\r\nfell asleep congratulating himself that nobody knew of his escapade. He\r\nwas not aware that the gently-snoring Sid was awake, and had been so for\r\nan hour.\r\n\r\nWhen Tom awoke, Sid was dressed and gone. There was a late look in the\r\nlight, a late sense in the atmosphere. He was startled. Why had he not\r\nbeen called--persecuted till he was up, as usual? The thought filled\r\nhim with bodings. Within five minutes he was dressed and down-stairs,\r\nfeeling sore and drowsy. The family were still at table, but they had\r\nfinished breakfast. There was no voice of rebuke; but there were averted\r\neyes; there was a silence and an air of solemnity that struck a chill\r\nto the culprit\xe2\x80\x99s heart. He sat down and tried to seem gay, but it\r\nwas up-hill work; it roused no smile, no response, and he lapsed into\r\nsilence and let his heart sink down to the depths.\r\n\r\nAfter breakfast his aunt took him aside, and Tom almost brightened in\r\nthe hope that he was going to be flogged; but it was not so. His aunt\r\nwept over him and asked him how he could go and break her old heart so;\r\nand finally told him to go on, and ruin himself and bring her gray hairs\r\nwith sorrow to the grave, for it was no use for her to try any more.\r\nThis was worse than a thousand whippings, and Tom\xe2\x80\x99s heart was sorer now\r\nthan his body. He cried, he pleaded for forgiveness, promised to reform\r\nover and over again, and then received his dismissal, feeling that\r\nhe had won but an imperfect forgiveness and established but a feeble\r\nconfidence.\r\n\r\nHe left the presence too miserable to even feel revengeful toward\r\nSid; and so the latter\xe2\x80\x99s prompt retreat through the back gate was\r\nunnecessary. He moped to school gloomy and sad, and took his flogging,\r\nalong with Joe Harper, for playing hookey the day before, with the\r\nair of one whose heart was busy with heavier woes and wholly dead to\r\ntrifles. Then he betook himself to his seat, rested his elbows on his\r\ndesk and his jaws in his hands, and stared at the wall with the stony\r\nstare of suffering that has reached the limit and can no further go.\r\nHis elbow was pressing against some hard substance. After a long time\r\nhe slowly and sadly changed his position, and took up this object with\r\na sigh. It was in a paper. He unrolled it. A long, lingering, colossal\r\nsigh followed, and his heart broke. It was his brass andiron knob!\r\n\r\nThis final feather broke the camel\xe2\x80\x99s back.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI\r\n\r\nCLOSE upon the hour of noon the whole village was suddenly electrified\r\nwith the ghastly news. No need of the as yet un-dreamed-of telegraph;\r\nthe tale flew from man to man, from group to group, from house to house,\r\nwith little less than telegraphic speed. Of course the schoolmaster gave\r\nholi-day for that afternoon; the town would have thought strangely of\r\nhim if he had not.\r\n\r\nA gory knife had been found close to the murdered man, and it had been\r\nrecognized by somebody as belonging to Muff Potter--so the story ran. And\r\nit was said that a belated citizen had come upon Potter washing himself\r\nin the \xe2\x80\x9cbranch\xe2\x80\x9d about one or two o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning, and that Potter\r\nhad at once sneaked off--suspicious circumstances, especially the washing\r\nwhich was not a habit with Potter. It was also said that the town had\r\nbeen ransacked for this \xe2\x80\x9cmurderer\xe2\x80\x9d (the public are not slow in the\r\nmatter of sifting evidence and arriving at a verdict), but that he\r\ncould not be found. Horsemen had departed down all the roads in every\r\ndirection, and the Sheriff \xe2\x80\x9cwas confident\xe2\x80\x9d that he would be captured\r\nbefore night.\r\n\r\nAll the town was drifting toward the graveyard. Tom\xe2\x80\x99s heartbreak\r\nvanished and he joined the procession, not because he would not\r\na thousand times rather go anywhere else, but because an awful,\r\nunaccountable fascination drew him on. Arrived at the dreadful place, he\r\nwormed his small body through the crowd and saw the dismal spectacle.\r\nIt seemed to him an age since he was there before. Somebody pinched\r\nhis arm. He turned, and his eyes met Huckleberry\xe2\x80\x99s. Then both looked\r\nelsewhere at once, and wondered if anybody had noticed anything in their\r\nmutual glance. But everybody was talking, and intent upon the grisly\r\nspectacle before them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cPoor young fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cThis ought to be a lesson to grave\r\nrobbers!\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cMuff Potter\xe2\x80\x99ll hang for this if they catch him!\xe2\x80\x9d This was the\r\ndrift of remark; and the minister said, \xe2\x80\x9cIt was a judgment; His hand is\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow Tom shivered from head to heel; for his eye fell upon the stolid\r\nface of Injun Joe. At this moment the crowd began to sway and struggle,\r\nand voices shouted, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s him! it\xe2\x80\x99s him! he\xe2\x80\x99s coming himself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho?
