Next he sought Ben Rogers; he had gone fishing.\r\nPresently he stumbled upon Huck Finn the Red-Handed. Huck would\r\nanswer. Tom took him to a private place and opened the matter to him\r\nconfidentially. Huck was willing. Huck was always willing to take a hand\r\nin any enterprise that offered entertainment and required no capital,\r\nfor he had a troublesome superabundance of that sort of time which is\r\nnot money. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99ll we dig?\xe2\x80\x9d said Huck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, most anywhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, is it hid all around?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed it ain\xe2\x80\x99t. It\xe2\x80\x99s hid in mighty particular places,\r\nHuck--sometimes on islands, sometimes in rotten chests under the end of\r\na limb of an old dead tree, just where the shadow falls at midnight; but\r\nmostly under the floor in ha\xe2\x80\x99nted houses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho hides it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, robbers, of course--who\xe2\x80\x99d you reckon? Sunday-school\r\nsup\xe2\x80\x99rintendents?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. If \xe2\x80\x99twas mine I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t hide it; I\xe2\x80\x99d spend it and have a\r\ngood time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo would I. But robbers don\xe2\x80\x99t do that way. They always hide it and\r\nleave it there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t they come after it any more?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, they think they will, but they generally forget the marks, or else\r\nthey die. Anyway, it lays there a long time and gets rusty; and by and\r\nby somebody finds an old yellow paper that tells how to find the marks--a\r\npaper that\xe2\x80\x99s got to be ciphered over about a week because it\xe2\x80\x99s mostly\r\nsigns and hy\xe2\x80\x99roglyphics.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHyro--which?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHy\xe2\x80\x99roglyphics--pictures and things, you know, that don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to mean\r\nanything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you got one of them papers, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, how you going to find the marks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want any marks. They always bury it under a ha\xe2\x80\x99nted house or on\r\nan island, or under a dead tree that\xe2\x80\x99s got one limb sticking out. Well,\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ve tried Jackson\xe2\x80\x99s Island a little, and we can try it again some\r\ntime; and there\xe2\x80\x99s the old ha\xe2\x80\x99nted house up the Still-House branch, and\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s lots of dead-limb trees--dead loads of \xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it under all of them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow you talk! No!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen how you going to know which one to go for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo for all of \xe2\x80\x99em!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Tom, it\xe2\x80\x99ll take all summer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what of that? Suppose you find a brass pot with a hundred dollars\r\nin it, all rusty and gray, or rotten chest full of di\xe2\x80\x99monds. How\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck\xe2\x80\x99s eyes glowed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s bully. Plenty bully enough for me. Just you gimme the hundred\r\ndollars and I don\xe2\x80\x99t want no di\xe2\x80\x99monds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. But I bet you I ain\xe2\x80\x99t going to throw off on di\xe2\x80\x99monds. Some\r\nof \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x99s worth twenty dollars apiece--there ain\xe2\x80\x99t any, hardly, but\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nworth six bits or a dollar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! Is that so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCert\xe2\x80\x99nly--anybody\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you so. Hain\xe2\x80\x99t you ever seen one, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot as I remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, kings have slathers of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99 know no kings, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon you don\xe2\x80\x99t. But if you was to go to Europe you\xe2\x80\x99d see a raft of\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em hopping around.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo they hop?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHop?--your granny! No!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what did you say they did, for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShucks, I only meant you\xe2\x80\x99d _see_ \xe2\x80\x99em--not hopping, of course--what do\r\nthey want to hop for?--but I mean you\xe2\x80\x99d just see \xe2\x80\x99em--scattered around,\r\nyou know, in a kind of a general way. Like that old humpbacked Richard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRichard? What\xe2\x80\x99s his other name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe didn\xe2\x80\x99t have any other name. Kings don\xe2\x80\x99t have any but a given name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut they don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if they like it, Tom, all right; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be a king\r\nand have only just a given name, like a nigger. But say--where you going\r\nto dig first?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. S\xe2\x80\x99pose we tackle that old dead-limb tree on the\r\nhill t\xe2\x80\x99other side of Still-House branch?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m agreed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo they got a crippled pick and a shovel, and set out on their\r\nthree-mile tramp. They arrived hot and panting, and threw themselves\r\ndown in the shade of a neighboring elm to rest and have a smoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like this,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo do I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, Huck, if we find a treasure here, what you going to do with your\r\nshare?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll have pie and a glass of soda every day, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll go to every\r\ncircus that comes along. I bet I\xe2\x80\x99ll have a gay time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, ain\xe2\x80\x99t you going to save any of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSave it? What for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, so as to have something to live on, by and by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that ain\xe2\x80\x99t any use. Pap would come back to thish-yer town some day\r\nand get his claws on it if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t hurry up, and I tell you he\xe2\x80\x99d clean\r\nit out pretty quick. What you going to do with yourn, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to buy a new drum, and a sure\xe2\x80\x99nough sword, and a red necktie\r\nand a bull pup, and get married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarried!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, you--why, you ain\xe2\x80\x99t in your right mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait--you\xe2\x80\x99ll see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s the foolishest thing you could do. Look at pap and my\r\nmother. Fight! Why, they used to fight all the time. I remember, mighty\r\nwell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat ain\xe2\x80\x99t anything. The girl I\xe2\x80\x99m going to marry won\xe2\x80\x99t fight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, I reckon they\xe2\x80\x99re all alike. They\xe2\x80\x99ll all comb a body. Now you\r\nbetter think \xe2\x80\x99bout this awhile. I tell you you better. What\xe2\x80\x99s the name\r\nof the gal?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt ain\xe2\x80\x99t a gal at all--it\xe2\x80\x99s a girl.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all the same, I reckon; some says gal, some says girl--both\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nright, like enough. Anyway, what\xe2\x80\x99s her name, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you some time--not now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right--that\xe2\x80\x99ll do. Only if you get married I\xe2\x80\x99ll be more lonesomer\r\nthan ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo you won\xe2\x80\x99t. You\xe2\x80\x99ll come and live with me. Now stir out of this and\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll go to digging.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey worked and sweated for half an hour. No result. They toiled another\r\nhalfhour. Still no result. Huck said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo they always bury it as deep as this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSometimes--not always. Not generally. I reckon we haven\xe2\x80\x99t got the right\r\nplace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo they chose a new spot and began again. The labor dragged a little,\r\nbut still they made progress. They pegged away in silence for some time.\r\nFinally Huck leaned on his shovel, swabbed the beaded drops from his\r\nbrow with his sleeve, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere you going to dig next, after we get this one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon maybe we\xe2\x80\x99ll tackle the old tree that\xe2\x80\x99s over yonder on Cardiff\r\nHill back of the widow\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon that\xe2\x80\x99ll be a good one. But won\xe2\x80\x99t the widow take it away from\r\nus, Tom? It\xe2\x80\x99s on her land.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_She_ take it away! Maybe she\xe2\x80\x99d like to try it once. Whoever finds one\r\nof these hid treasures, it belongs to him. It don\xe2\x80\x99t make any difference\r\nwhose land it\xe2\x80\x99s on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was satisfactory. The work went on. By and by Huck said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlame it, we must be in the wrong place again. What do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is mighty curious, Huck. I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand it. Sometimes witches\r\ninterfere. I reckon maybe that\xe2\x80\x99s what\xe2\x80\x99s the trouble now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShucks! Witches ain\xe2\x80\x99t got no power in the daytime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s so. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think of that. Oh, I know what the matter is!\r\nWhat a blamed lot of fools we are! You got to find out where the shadow\r\nof the limb falls at midnight, and that\xe2\x80\x99s where you dig!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen consound it, we\xe2\x80\x99ve fooled away all this work for nothing. Now hang\r\nit all, we got to come back in the night. It\xe2\x80\x99s an awful long way. Can\r\nyou get out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bet I will. We\xe2\x80\x99ve got to do it tonight, too, because if somebody sees\r\nthese holes they\xe2\x80\x99ll know in a minute what\xe2\x80\x99s here and they\xe2\x80\x99ll go for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll come around and maow tonight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. Let\xe2\x80\x99s hide the tools in the bushes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boys were there that night, about the appointed time. They sat in\r\nthe shadow waiting. It was a lonely place, and an hour made solemn by\r\nold traditions. Spirits whispered in the rustling leaves, ghosts lurked\r\nin the murky nooks, the deep baying of a hound floated up out of the\r\ndistance, an owl answered with his sepulchral note. The boys were\r\nsubdued by these solemnities, and talked little. By and by they judged\r\nthat twelve had come; they marked where the shadow fell, and began to\r\ndig. Their hopes commenced to rise. Their interest grew stronger, and\r\ntheir industry kept pace with it. The hole deepened and still deepened,\r\nbut every time their hearts jumped to hear the pick strike upon\r\nsomething, they only suffered a new disappointment. It was only a stone\r\nor a chunk. At last Tom said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt ain\xe2\x80\x99t any use, Huck, we\xe2\x80\x99re wrong again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, but we _can\xe2\x80\x99t_ be wrong. We spotted the shadder to a dot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it, but then there\xe2\x80\x99s another thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, we only guessed at the time. Like enough it was too late or too\r\nearly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck dropped his shovel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s it,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the very trouble. We got to give this one\r\nup. We can\xe2\x80\x99t ever tell the right time, and besides this kind of thing\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntoo awful, here this time of night with witches and ghosts a-fluttering\r\naround so. I feel as if something\xe2\x80\x99s behind me all the time; \xc2\xa0and I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nafeard to turn around, becuz maybe there\xe2\x80\x99s others in front a-waiting for\r\na chance. I been creeping all over, ever since I got here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ve been pretty much so, too, Huck. They most always put in a\r\ndead man when they bury a treasure under a tree, to look out for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLordy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, they do. I\xe2\x80\x99ve always heard that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, I don\xe2\x80\x99t like to fool around much where there\xe2\x80\x99s dead people. A\r\nbody\xe2\x80\x99s bound to get into trouble with \xe2\x80\x99em, sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like to stir \xe2\x80\x99em up, either. S\xe2\x80\x99pose this one here was to stick\r\nhis skull out and say something!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t Tom! It\xe2\x80\x99s awful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it just is. Huck, I don\xe2\x80\x99t feel comfortable a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, Tom, let\xe2\x80\x99s give this place up, and try somewheres else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, I reckon we better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99ll it be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom considered awhile; and then said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe ha\xe2\x80\x99nted house. That\xe2\x80\x99s it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlame it, I don\xe2\x80\x99t like ha\xe2\x80\x99nted houses, Tom. Why, they\xe2\x80\x99re a dern sight\r\nworse\xe2\x80\x99n dead people. Dead people might talk, maybe, but they don\xe2\x80\x99t come\r\nsliding around in a shroud, when you ain\xe2\x80\x99t noticing, and peep over your\r\nshoulder all of a sudden and grit their teeth, the way a ghost does. I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t stand such a thing as that, Tom--nobody could.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but, Huck, ghosts don\xe2\x80\x99t travel around only at night. They won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhender us from digging there in the daytime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s so. But you know mighty well people don\xe2\x80\x99t go about that\r\nha\xe2\x80\x99nted house in the day nor the night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s mostly because they don\xe2\x80\x99t like to go where a man\xe2\x80\x99s been\r\nmurdered, anyway--but nothing\xe2\x80\x99s ever been seen around that house except\r\nin the night--just some blue lights slipping by the windows--no regular\r\nghosts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, where you see one of them blue lights flickering around, Tom,\r\nyou can bet there\xe2\x80\x99s a ghost mighty close behind it. It stands to reason.\r\nBecuz you know that they don\xe2\x80\x99t anybody but ghosts use \xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s so. But anyway they don\xe2\x80\x99t come around in the daytime, so\r\nwhat\xe2\x80\x99s the use of our being afeard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, all right. We\xe2\x80\x99ll tackle the ha\xe2\x80\x99nted house if you say so--but I\r\nreckon it\xe2\x80\x99s taking chances.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had started down the hill by this time. There in the middle of the\r\nmoonlit valley below them stood the \xe2\x80\x9cha\xe2\x80\x99nted\xe2\x80\x9d house, utterly isolated,\r\nits fences gone long ago, rank weeds smothering the very doorsteps, the\r\nchimney crumbled to ruin, the window-sashes vacant, a corner of the roof\r\ncaved in. The boys gazed awhile, half expecting to see a blue light flit\r\npast a window; then talking in a low tone, as befitted the time and the\r\ncircumstances, they struck far off to the right, to give the haunted\r\nhouse a wide berth, and took their way homeward through the woods that\r\nadorned the rearward side of Cardiff Hill.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVI\r\n\r\nABOUT noon the next day the boys arrived at the dead tree; they had come\r\nfor their tools. Tom was impatient to go to the haunted house; Huck was\r\nmeasurably so, also--but suddenly said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLookyhere, Tom, do you know what day it is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom mentally ran over the days of the week, and then quickly lifted his\r\neyes with a startled look in them--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy! I never once thought of it, Huck!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t neither, but all at once it popped onto me that it was\r\nFriday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlame it, a body can\xe2\x80\x99t be too careful, Huck. We might \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 got into an\r\nawful scrape, tackling such a thing on a Friday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Might_! Better say we _would_! There\xe2\x80\x99s some lucky days, maybe, but\r\nFriday ain\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny fool knows that. I don\xe2\x80\x99t reckon _you_ was the first that found it\r\nout, Huck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I never said I was, did I? And Friday ain\xe2\x80\x99t all, neither. I had a\r\nrotten bad dream last night--dreampt about rats.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! Sure sign of trouble. Did they fight?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s good, Huck. When they don\xe2\x80\x99t fight it\xe2\x80\x99s only a sign that\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s trouble around, you know. All we got to do is to look mighty\r\nsharp and keep out of it. We\xe2\x80\x99ll drop this thing for today, and play. Do\r\nyou know Robin Hood, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Who\xe2\x80\x99s Robin Hood?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, he was one of the greatest men that was ever in England--and the\r\nbest. He was a robber.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCracky, I wisht I was. Who did he rob?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly sheriffs and bishops and rich people and kings, and such like. But\r\nhe never bothered the poor. He loved \xe2\x80\x99em. He always divided up with \xe2\x80\x99em\r\nperfectly square.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he must \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 been a brick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bet you he was, Huck. Oh, he was the noblest man that ever was.\r\nThey ain\xe2\x80\x99t any such men now, I can tell you. He could lick any man in\r\nEngland, with one hand tied behind him; and he could take his yew bow\r\nand plug a ten-cent piece every time, a mile and a half.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s a _yew_ bow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. It\xe2\x80\x99s some kind of a bow, of course. And if he hit that\r\ndime only on the edge he would set down and cry--and curse. But we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nplay Robin Hood--it\xe2\x80\x99s nobby fun. I\xe2\x80\x99ll learn you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m agreed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo they played Robin Hood all the afternoon, now and then casting a\r\nyearning eye down upon the haunted house and passing a remark about the\r\nmorrow\xe2\x80\x99s prospects and possibilities there. As the sun began to sink\r\ninto the west they took their way homeward athwart the long shadows\r\nof the trees and soon were buried from sight in the forests of Cardiff\r\nHill.\r\n\r\nOn Saturday, shortly after noon, the boys were at the dead tree again.\r\nThey had a smoke and a chat in the shade, and then dug a little in their\r\nlast hole, not with great hope, but merely because Tom said there were\r\nso many cases where people had given up a treasure after getting down\r\nwithin six inches of it, and then somebody else had come along and\r\nturned it up with a single thrust of a shovel. The thing failed this\r\ntime, however, so the boys shouldered their tools and went away feeling\r\nthat they had not trifled with fortune, but had fulfilled all the\r\nrequirements that belong to the business of treasure-hunting.\r\n\r\nWhen they reached the haunted house there was something so weird and\r\ngrisly about the dead silence that reigned there under the baking sun,\r\nand something so depressing about the loneliness and desolation of the\r\nplace, that they were afraid, for a moment, to venture in. Then they\r\ncrept to the door and took a trembling peep. They saw a weedgrown,\r\nfloorless room, unplastered, an ancient fireplace, vacant windows,\r\na ruinous staircase; and here, there, and everywhere hung ragged and\r\nabandoned cobwebs. They presently entered, softly, with quickened\r\npulses, talking in whispers, ears alert to catch the slightest sound,\r\nand muscles tense and ready for instant retreat.\r\n\r\nIn a little while familiarity modified their fears and they gave the\r\nplace a critical and interested examination, rather admiring their own\r\nboldness, and wondering at it, too. Next they wanted to look upstairs.\r\nThis was something like cutting off retreat, but they got to daring\r\neach other, and of course there could be but one result--they threw their\r\ntools into a corner and made the ascent. Up there were the same signs of\r\ndecay. In one corner they found a closet that promised mystery, but the\r\npromise was a fraud--there was nothing in it. Their courage was up now\r\nand well in hand. They were about to go down and begin work when--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Huck, blanching with fright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSh!... There!... Hear it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes!... Oh, my! Let\xe2\x80\x99s run!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep still! Don\xe2\x80\x99t you budge! They\xe2\x80\x99re coming right toward the door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boys stretched themselves upon the floor with their eyes to\r\nknotholes in the planking, and lay waiting, in a misery of fear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ve stopped.... No--coming.... Here they are. Don\xe2\x80\x99t whisper another\r\nword, Huck. My goodness, I wish I was out of this!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTwo men entered. Each boy said to himself: \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s the old deaf and\r\ndumb Spaniard that\xe2\x80\x99s been about town once or twice lately--never saw\r\nt\xe2\x80\x99other man before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cT\xe2\x80\x99other\xe2\x80\x9d was a ragged, unkempt creature, with nothing very pleasant\r\nin his face. The Spaniard was wrapped in a serape; he had bushy white\r\nwhiskers; long white hair flowed from under his sombrero, and he wore\r\ngreen goggles. When they came in, \xe2\x80\x9ct\xe2\x80\x99other\xe2\x80\x9d was talking in a low voice;\r\nthey sat down on the ground, facing the door, with their backs to the\r\nwall, and the speaker continued his remarks. His manner became less\r\nguarded and his words more distinct as he proceeded:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve thought it all over, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t like it. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndangerous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDangerous!\xe2\x80\x9d grunted the \xe2\x80\x9cdeaf and dumb\xe2\x80\x9d Spaniard--to the vast surprise\r\nof the boys. \xe2\x80\x9cMilksop!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis voice made the boys gasp and quake. It was Injun Joe\xe2\x80\x99s! There was\r\nsilence for some time. Then Joe said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s any more dangerous than that job up yonder--but nothing\xe2\x80\x99s come of\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s different. Away up the river so, and not another house about.\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Twon\xe2\x80\x99t ever be known that we tried, anyway, long as we didn\xe2\x80\x99t succeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what\xe2\x80\x99s more dangerous than coming here in the daytime!--anybody\r\nwould suspicion us that saw us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that. But there warn\xe2\x80\x99t any other place as handy after that fool\r\nof a job. I want to quit this shanty. I wanted to yesterday, only it\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t any use trying to stir out of here, with those infernal boys\r\nplaying over there on the hill right in full view.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose infernal boys\xe2\x80\x9d quaked again under the inspiration of this remark,\r\nand thought how lucky it was that they had remembered it was Friday and\r\nconcluded to wait a day. They wished in their hearts they had waited a\r\nyear.\r\n\r\nThe two men got out some food and made a luncheon. After a long and\r\nthoughtful silence, Injun Joe said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, lad--you go back up the river where you belong. Wait there\r\ntill you hear from me. I\xe2\x80\x99ll take the chances on dropping into this town\r\njust once more, for a look. We\xe2\x80\x99ll do that \xe2\x80\x98dangerous\xe2\x80\x99 job after I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nspied around a little and think things look well for it. Then for Texas!\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99ll leg it together!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was satisfactory. Both men presently fell to yawning, and Injun Joe\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m dead for sleep! It\xe2\x80\x99s your turn to watch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe curled down in the weeds and soon began to snore. His comrade stirred\r\nhim once or twice and he became quiet. Presently the watcher began to\r\nnod; his head drooped lower and lower, both men began to snore now.\r\n\r\nThe boys drew a long, grateful breath. Tom whispered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow\xe2\x80\x99s our chance--come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t--I\xe2\x80\x99d die if they was to wake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom urged--Huck held back. At last Tom rose slowly and softly, and\r\nstarted alone. But the first step he made wrung such a hideous creak\r\nfrom the crazy floor that he sank down almost dead with fright. He never\r\nmade a second attempt. The boys lay there counting the dragging moments\r\ntill it seemed to them that time must be done and eternity growing gray;\r\nand then they were grateful to note that at last the sun was setting.\r\n\r\nNow one snore ceased. Injun Joe sat up, stared around--smiled grimly upon\r\nhis comrade, whose head was drooping upon his knees--stirred him up with\r\nhis foot and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere! _You\xe2\x80\x99re_ a watchman, ain\xe2\x80\x99t you! All right, though--nothing\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhappened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy! have I been asleep?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, partly, partly. Nearly time for us to be moving, pard. What\xe2\x80\x99ll we\r\ndo with what little swag we\xe2\x80\x99ve got left?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know--leave it here as we\xe2\x80\x99ve always done, I reckon. No use to\r\ntake it away till we start south. Six hundred and fifty in silver\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsomething to carry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell--all right--it won\xe2\x80\x99t matter to come here once more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo--but I\xe2\x80\x99d say come in the night as we used to do--it\xe2\x80\x99s better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes: but look here; it may be a good while before I get the right\r\nchance at that job; accidents might happen; \xe2\x80\x99tain\xe2\x80\x99t in such a very good\r\nplace; we\xe2\x80\x99ll just regularly bury it--and bury it deep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood idea,\xe2\x80\x9d said the comrade, who walked across the room, knelt down,\r\nraised one of the rearward hearth-stones and took out a bag that jingled\r\npleasantly. He subtracted from it twenty or thirty dollars for himself\r\nand as much for Injun Joe, and passed the bag to the latter, who was on\r\nhis knees in the corner, now, digging with his bowie-knife.\r\n\r\nThe boys forgot all their fears, all their miseries in an instant. With\r\ngloating eyes they watched every movement. Luck!--the splendor of it was\r\nbeyond all imagination! Six hundred dollars was money enough to make\r\nhalf a dozen boys rich! Here was treasure-hunting under the happiest\r\nauspices--there would not be any bothersome uncertainty as to where to\r\ndig. They nudged each other every moment--eloquent nudges and easily\r\nunderstood, for they simply meant--\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but ain\xe2\x80\x99t you glad _now_ we\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nhere!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJoe\xe2\x80\x99s knife struck upon something.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello!\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d said his comrade.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalf-rotten plank--no, it\xe2\x80\x99s a box, I believe. Here--bear a hand and we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nsee what it\xe2\x80\x99s here for. Never mind, I\xe2\x80\x99ve broke a hole.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe reached his hand in and drew it out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMan, it\xe2\x80\x99s money!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two men examined the handful of coins. They were gold. The boys\r\nabove were as excited as themselves, and as delighted.\r\n\r\nJoe\xe2\x80\x99s comrade said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll make quick work of this. There\xe2\x80\x99s an old rusty pick over amongst\r\nthe weeds in the corner the other side of the fireplace--I saw it a\r\nminute ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe ran and brought the boys\xe2\x80\x99 pick and shovel. Injun Joe took the\r\npick, looked it over critically, shook his head, muttered something to\r\nhimself, and then began to use it. The box was soon unearthed. It was\r\nnot very large; it was iron bound and had been very strong before the\r\nslow years had injured it. The men contemplated the treasure awhile in\r\nblissful silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPard, there\xe2\x80\x99s thousands of dollars here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Injun Joe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twas always said that Murrel\xe2\x80\x99s gang used to be around here one\r\nsummer,\xe2\x80\x9d the stranger observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Injun Joe; \xe2\x80\x9cand this looks like it, I should say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you won\xe2\x80\x99t need to do that job.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe halfbreed frowned. Said he:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know me. Least you don\xe2\x80\x99t know all about that thing. \xe2\x80\x99Tain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nrobbery altogether--it\xe2\x80\x99s _revenge_!\xe2\x80\x9d and a wicked light flamed in his\r\neyes. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll need your help in it. When it\xe2\x80\x99s finished--then Texas. Go home\r\nto your Nance and your kids, and stand by till you hear from me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell--if you say so; what\xe2\x80\x99ll we do with this--bury it again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. [Ravishing delight overhead.] _No_! by the great Sachem, no!\r\n[Profound distress overhead.] I\xe2\x80\x99d nearly forgot. That pick had fresh\r\nearth on it! [The boys were sick with terror in a moment.] What business\r\nhas a pick and a shovel here? What business with fresh earth on\r\nthem? Who brought them here--and where are they gone? Have you heard\r\nanybody?--seen anybody? What! bury it again and leave them to come and\r\nsee the ground disturbed? Not exactly--not exactly. We\xe2\x80\x99ll take it to my\r\nden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course! Might have thought of that before. You mean Number\r\nOne?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo--Number Two--under the cross. The other place is bad--too common.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. It\xe2\x80\x99s nearly dark enough to start.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInjun Joe got up and went about from window to window cautiously peeping\r\nout. Presently he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho could have brought those tools here? Do you reckon they can be\r\nupstairs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boys\xe2\x80\x99 breath forsook them. Injun Joe put his hand on his knife,\r\nhalted a moment, undecided, and then turned toward the stairway. The\r\nboys thought of the closet, but their strength was gone. The steps came\r\ncreaking up the stairs--the intolerable distress of the situation woke\r\nthe stricken resolution of the lads--they were about to spring for the\r\ncloset, when there was a crash of rotten timbers and Injun Joe landed on\r\nthe ground amid the debris of the ruined stairway. He gathered himself\r\nup cursing, and his comrade said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow what\xe2\x80\x99s the use of all that? If it\xe2\x80\x99s anybody, and they\xe2\x80\x99re up there,\r\nlet them _stay_ there--who cares? If they want to jump down, now, and get\r\ninto trouble, who objects? It will be dark in fifteen minutes--and then\r\nlet them follow us if they want to. I\xe2\x80\x99m willing. In my opinion, whoever\r\nhove those things in here caught a sight of us and took us for ghosts or\r\ndevils or something. I\xe2\x80\x99ll bet they\xe2\x80\x99re running yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJoe grumbled awhile; then he agreed with his friend that what daylight\r\nwas left ought to be economized in getting things ready for leaving.\r\nShortly afterward they slipped out of the house in the deepening\r\ntwilight, and moved toward the river with their precious box.\r\n\r\nTom and Huck rose up, weak but vastly relieved, and stared after them\r\nthrough the chinks between the logs of the house. Follow? Not they. They\r\nwere content to reach ground again without broken necks, and take the\r\ntownward track over the hill. They did not talk much. They were too much\r\nabsorbed in hating themselves--hating the ill luck that made them take\r\nthe spade and the pick there. But for that, Injun Joe never would have\r\nsuspected. He would have hidden the silver with the gold to wait\r\nthere till his \xe2\x80\x9crevenge\xe2\x80\x9d was satisfied, and then he would have had the\r\nmisfortune to find that money turn up missing. Bitter, bitter luck that\r\nthe tools were ever brought there!\r\n\r\nThey resolved to keep a lookout for that Spaniard when he should come to\r\ntown spying out for chances to do his revengeful job, and follow him to\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNumber Two,\xe2\x80\x9d wherever that might be. Then a ghastly thought occurred to\r\nTom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRevenge? What if he means _us_, Huck!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d said Huck, nearly fainting.\r\n\r\nThey talked it all over, and as they entered town they agreed to believe\r\nthat he might possibly mean somebody else--at least that he might at\r\nleast mean nobody but Tom, since only Tom had testified.\r\n\r\nVery, very small comfort it was to Tom to be alone in danger! Company\r\nwould be a palpable improvement, he thought.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVII\r\n\r\nTHE adventure of the day mightily tormented Tom\xe2\x80\x99s dreams that night.\r\nFour times he had his hands on that rich treasure and four times\r\nit wasted to nothingness in his fingers as sleep forsook him and\r\nwakefulness brought back the hard reality of his misfortune. As he lay\r\nin the early morning recalling the incidents of his great adventure, he\r\nnoticed that they seemed curiously subdued and far away--somewhat as if\r\nthey had happened in another world, or in a time long gone by. Then it\r\noccurred to him that the great adventure itself must be a dream! There\r\nwas one very strong argument in favor of this idea--namely, that the\r\nquantity of coin he had seen was too vast to be real. He had never seen\r\nas much as fifty dollars in one mass before, and he was like all boys of\r\nhis age and station in life, in that he imagined that all references to\r\n\xe2\x80\x9chundreds\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cthousands\xe2\x80\x9d were mere fanciful forms of speech, and that\r\nno such sums really existed in the world. He never had supposed for\r\na moment that so large a sum as a hundred dollars was to be found in\r\nactual money in any one\xe2\x80\x99s possession. If his notions of hidden treasure\r\nhad been analyzed, they would have been found to consist of a handful of\r\nreal dimes and a bushel of vague, splendid, ungraspable dollars.\r\n\r\nBut the incidents of his adventure grew sensibly sharper and clearer\r\nunder the attrition of thinking them over, and so he presently found\r\nhimself leaning to the impression that the thing might not have been a\r\ndream, after all. This uncertainty must be swept away. He would snatch a\r\nhurried breakfast and go and find Huck. Huck was sitting on the gunwale\r\nof a flatboat, listlessly dangling his feet in the water and looking\r\nvery melancholy. Tom concluded to let Huck lead up to the subject. If\r\nhe did not do it, then the adventure would be proved to have been only a\r\ndream.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello, Huck!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello, yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSilence, for a minute.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, if we\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 left the blame tools at the dead tree, we\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 got\r\nthe money. Oh, ain\xe2\x80\x99t it awful!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tain\xe2\x80\x99t a dream, then, \xe2\x80\x99tain\xe2\x80\x99t a dream! Somehow I most wish it was.\r\nDog\xe2\x80\x99d if I don\xe2\x80\x99t, Huck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat ain\xe2\x80\x99t a dream?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that thing yesterday. I been half thinking it was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDream! If them stairs hadn\xe2\x80\x99t broke down you\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 seen how much dream\r\nit was! I\xe2\x80\x99ve had dreams enough all night--with that patch-eyed Spanish\r\ndevil going for me all through \xe2\x80\x99em--rot him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not rot him. _Find_ him! Track the money!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, we\xe2\x80\x99ll never find him. A feller don\xe2\x80\x99t have only one chance for such\r\na pile--and that one\xe2\x80\x99s lost. I\xe2\x80\x99d feel mighty shaky if I was to see him,\r\nanyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, so\xe2\x80\x99d I; but I\xe2\x80\x99d like to see him, anyway--and track him out--to his\r\nNumber Two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNumber Two--yes, that\xe2\x80\x99s it. I been thinking \xe2\x80\x99bout that. But I can\xe2\x80\x99t make\r\nnothing out of it. What do you reckon it is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dono. It\xe2\x80\x99s too deep. Say, Huck--maybe it\xe2\x80\x99s the number of a house!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoody!... No, Tom, that ain\xe2\x80\x99t it. If it is, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t in this one-horse\r\ntown. They ain\xe2\x80\x99t no numbers here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s so. Lemme think a minute. Here--it\xe2\x80\x99s the number of a\r\nroom--in a tavern, you know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s the trick! They ain\xe2\x80\x99t only two taverns. We can find out\r\nquick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou stay here, Huck, till I come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom was off at once. He did not care to have Huck\xe2\x80\x99s company in public\r\nplaces. He was gone half an hour. He found that in the best tavern, No.\r\n2 had long been occupied by a young lawyer, and was still so occupied.\r\nIn the less ostentatious house, No. 2 was a mystery. The tavern-keeper\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nyoung son said it was kept locked all the time, and he never saw anybody\r\ngo into it or come out of it except at night; he did not know any\r\nparticular reason for this state of things; had had some little\r\ncuriosity, but it was rather feeble; had made the most of the mystery\r\nby entertaining himself with the idea that that room was \xe2\x80\x9cha\xe2\x80\x99nted\xe2\x80\x9d; had\r\nnoticed that there was a light in there the night before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I\xe2\x80\x99ve found out, Huck. I reckon that\xe2\x80\x99s the very No. 2 we\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nafter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon it is, Tom. Now what you going to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLemme think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom thought a long time. Then he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you. The back door of that No. 2 is the door that comes out\r\ninto that little close alley between the tavern and the old rattle trap\r\nof a brick store. Now you get hold of all the doorkeys you can find, and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll nip all of auntie\xe2\x80\x99s, and the first dark night we\xe2\x80\x99ll go there and\r\ntry \xe2\x80\x99em. And mind you, keep a lookout for Injun Joe, because he said he\r\nwas going to drop into town and spy around once more for a chance to get\r\nhis revenge. If you see him, you just follow him; and if he don\xe2\x80\x99t go to\r\nthat No. 2, that ain\xe2\x80\x99t the place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLordy, I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to foller him by myself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99ll be night, sure. He mightn\xe2\x80\x99t ever see you--and if he did,\r\nmaybe he\xe2\x80\x99d never think anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if it\xe2\x80\x99s pretty dark I reckon I\xe2\x80\x99ll track him. I dono--I dono. I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ntry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet I\xe2\x80\x99ll follow him, if it\xe2\x80\x99s dark, Huck. Why, he might \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 found\r\nout he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get his revenge, and be going right after that money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s so, Tom, it\xe2\x80\x99s so. I\xe2\x80\x99ll foller him; I will, by jingoes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you\xe2\x80\x99re _talking_! Don\xe2\x80\x99t you ever weaken, Huck, and I won\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVIII\r\n\r\nTHAT night Tom and Huck were ready for their adventure. They hung about\r\nthe neighborhood of the tavern until after nine, one watching the alley\r\nat a distance and the other the tavern door. Nobody entered the alley or\r\nleft it; nobody resembling the Spaniard entered or left the tavern\r\ndoor. The night promised to be a fair one; so Tom went home with the\r\nunderstanding that if a considerable degree of darkness came on, Huck\r\nwas to come and \xe2\x80\x9cmaow,\xe2\x80\x9d whereupon he would slip out and try the keys.\r\nBut the night remained clear, and Huck closed his watch and retired to\r\nbed in an empty sugar hogshead about twelve.\r\n\r\nTuesday the boys had the same ill luck. Also Wednesday. But Thursday\r\nnight promised better. Tom slipped out in good season with his aunt\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nold tin lantern, and a large towel to blindfold it with. He hid the\r\nlantern in Huck\xe2\x80\x99s sugar hogshead and the watch began. An hour before\r\nmidnight the tavern closed up and its lights (the only ones thereabouts)\r\nwere put out. No Spaniard had been seen. Nobody had entered or left the\r\nalley. Everything was auspicious. The blackness of darkness reigned,\r\nthe perfect stillness was interrupted only by occasional mutterings of\r\ndistant thunder.\r\n\r\nTom got his lantern, lit it in the hogshead, wrapped it closely in the\r\ntowel, and the two adventurers crept in the gloom toward the tavern.\r\nHuck stood sentry and Tom felt his way into the alley. Then there was\r\na season of waiting anxiety that weighed upon Huck\xe2\x80\x99s spirits like a\r\nmountain. He began to wish he could see a flash from the lantern--it\r\nwould frighten him, but it would at least tell him that Tom was alive\r\nyet. It seemed hours since Tom had disappeared. Surely he must have\r\nfainted; maybe he was dead; maybe his heart had burst under terror and\r\nexcitement. In his uneasiness Huck found himself drawing closer\r\nand closer to the alley; fearing all sorts of dreadful things, and\r\nmomentarily expecting some catastrophe to happen that would take away\r\nhis breath. There was not much to take away, for he seemed only able to\r\ninhale it by thimblefuls, and his heart would soon wear itself out, the\r\nway it was beating. Suddenly there was a flash of light and Tom came\r\ntearing by him: \xe2\x80\x9cRun!\xe2\x80\x9d said he; \xe2\x80\x9crun, for your life!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe needn\xe2\x80\x99t have repeated it; once was enough; Huck was making thirty or\r\nforty miles an hour before the repetition was uttered. The boys never\r\nstopped till they reached the shed of a deserted slaughter-house at the\r\nlower end of the village. Just as they got within its shelter the storm\r\nburst and the rain poured down. As soon as Tom got his breath he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck, it was awful! I tried two of the keys, just as soft as I could;\r\nbut they seemed to make such a power of racket that I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly\r\nget my breath I was so scared. They wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t turn in the lock, either.\r\nWell, without noticing what I was doing, I took hold of the knob, and\r\nopen comes the door! It warn\xe2\x80\x99t locked! I hopped in, and shook off the\r\ntowel, and, _Great Caesar\xe2\x80\x99s Ghost!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat!--what\xe2\x80\x99d you see, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck, I most stepped onto Injun Joe\xe2\x80\x99s hand!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes! He was lying there, sound asleep on the floor, with his old patch\r\non his eye and his arms spread out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLordy, what did you do? Did he wake up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, never budged. Drunk, I reckon. I just grabbed that towel and\r\nstarted!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d never \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 thought of the towel, I bet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I would. My aunt would make me mighty sick if I lost it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, Tom, did you see that box?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t wait to look around. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see the box, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see\r\nthe cross. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see anything but a bottle and a tin cup on the floor\r\nby Injun Joe; yes, I saw two barrels and lots more bottles in the room.\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t you see, now, what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with that ha\xe2\x80\x99nted room?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s ha\xe2\x80\x99nted with whiskey! Maybe _all_ the Temperance Taverns have\r\ngot a ha\xe2\x80\x99nted room, hey, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I reckon maybe that\xe2\x80\x99s so. Who\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 thought such a thing? But\r\nsay, Tom, now\xe2\x80\x99s a mighty good time to get that box, if Injun Joe\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndrunk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is, that! You try it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck shuddered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, no--I reckon not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I reckon not, Huck. Only one bottle alongside of Injun Joe ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nenough. If there\xe2\x80\x99d been three, he\xe2\x80\x99d be drunk enough and I\xe2\x80\x99d do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a long pause for reflection, and then Tom said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLookyhere, Huck, less not try that thing any more till we know Injun\r\nJoe\xe2\x80\x99s not in there. It\xe2\x80\x99s too scary. Now, if we watch every night, we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nbe dead sure to see him go out, some time or other, and then we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nsnatch that box quicker\xe2\x80\x99n lightning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99m agreed. I\xe2\x80\x99ll watch the whole night long, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it every\r\nnight, too, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll do the other part of the job.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, I will. All you got to do is to trot up Hooper Street a\r\nblock and maow--and if I\xe2\x80\x99m asleep, you throw some gravel at the window\r\nand that\xe2\x80\x99ll fetch me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAgreed, and good as wheat!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Huck, the storm\xe2\x80\x99s over, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll go home. It\xe2\x80\x99ll begin to be\r\ndaylight in a couple of hours. You go back and watch that long, will\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said I would, Tom, and I will. I\xe2\x80\x99ll ha\xe2\x80\x99nt that tavern every night for\r\na year! I\xe2\x80\x99ll sleep all day and I\xe2\x80\x99ll stand watch all night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all right. Now, where you going to sleep?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn Ben Rogers\xe2\x80\x99 hayloft. He lets me, and so does his pap\xe2\x80\x99s nigger man,\r\nUncle Jake. I tote water for Uncle Jake whenever he wants me to, and any\r\ntime I ask him he gives me a little something to eat if he can spare it.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s a mighty good nigger, Tom. He likes me, becuz I don\xe2\x80\x99t ever act as\r\nif I was above him. Sometime I\xe2\x80\x99ve set right down and eat _with_ him. But\r\nyou needn\xe2\x80\x99t tell that. A body\xe2\x80\x99s got to do things when he\xe2\x80\x99s awful hungry\r\nhe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t want to do as a steady thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if I don\xe2\x80\x99t want you in the daytime, I\xe2\x80\x99ll let you sleep. I won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncome bothering around. Any time you see something\xe2\x80\x99s up, in the night,\r\njust skip right around and maow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIX\r\n\r\nTHE first thing Tom heard on Friday morning was a glad piece of\r\nnews--Judge Thatcher\xe2\x80\x99s family had come back to town the night before.\r\nBoth Injun Joe and the treasure sunk into secondary importance for a\r\nmoment, and Becky took the chief place in the boy\xe2\x80\x99s interest. He saw her\r\nand they had an exhausting good time playing \xe2\x80\x9chispy\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cgully-keeper\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n with a crowd of their schoolmates. The day was completed and crowned in\r\na peculiarly satisfactory way: Becky teased her mother to appoint\r\nthe next day for the long-promised and long-delayed picnic, and she\r\nconsented. The child\xe2\x80\x99s delight was boundless; and Tom\xe2\x80\x99s not more\r\nmoderate. The invitations were sent out before sunset, and straightway\r\nthe young folks of the village were thrown into a fever of preparation\r\nand pleasurable anticipation. Tom\xe2\x80\x99s excitement enabled him to keep\r\nawake until a pretty late hour, and he had good hopes of hearing Huck\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cmaow,\xe2\x80\x9d and of having his treasure to astonish Becky and the picnickers\r\nwith, next day; but he was disappointed. No signal came that night.\r\n\r\nMorning came, eventually, and by ten or eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock a giddy and\r\nrollicking company were gathered at Judge Thatcher\xe2\x80\x99s, and everything was\r\nready for a start. It was not the custom for elderly people to mar the\r\npicnics with their presence. The children were considered safe enough\r\nunder the wings of a few young ladies of eighteen and a few young\r\ngentlemen of twenty-three or thereabouts. The old steam ferry-boat was\r\nchartered for the occasion; presently the gay throng filed up the main\r\nstreet laden with provision-baskets. Sid was sick and had to miss\r\nthe fun; Mary remained at home to entertain him. The last thing Mrs.\r\nThatcher said to Becky, was:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll not get back till late. Perhaps you\xe2\x80\x99d better stay all night with\r\nsome of the girls that live near the ferry-landing, child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I\xe2\x80\x99ll stay with Susy Harper, mamma.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. And mind and behave yourself and don\xe2\x80\x99t be any trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPresently, as they tripped along, Tom said to Becky:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay--I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what we\xe2\x80\x99ll do. \xe2\x80\x99Stead of going to Joe Harper\xe2\x80\x99s we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nclimb right up the hill and stop at the Widow Douglas\xe2\x80\x99. She\xe2\x80\x99ll have\r\nice-cream! She has it most every day--dead loads of it. And she\xe2\x80\x99ll be\r\nawful glad to have us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that will be fun!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Becky reflected a moment and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what will mamma say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99ll she ever know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe girl turned the idea over in her mind, and said reluctantly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon it\xe2\x80\x99s wrong--but--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut shucks! Your mother won\xe2\x80\x99t know, and so what\xe2\x80\x99s the harm? All she\r\nwants is that you\xe2\x80\x99ll be safe; and I bet you she\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 said go there if\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 thought of it. I know she would!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Widow Douglas\xe2\x80\x99 splendid hospitality was a tempting bait. It and\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s persuasions presently carried the day. So it was decided to say\r\nnothing to anybody about the night\xe2\x80\x99s programme. Presently it occurred to\r\nTom that maybe Huck might come this very night and give the signal. The\r\nthought took a deal of the spirit out of his anticipations. Still he\r\ncould not bear to give up the fun at Widow Douglas\xe2\x80\x99. And why should he\r\ngive it up, he reasoned--the signal did not come the night before, so\r\nwhy should it be any more likely to come tonight? The sure fun of the\r\nevening outweighed the uncertain treasure; and, boy-like, he determined\r\nto yield to the stronger inclination and not allow himself to think of\r\nthe box of money another time that day.\r\n\r\nThree miles below town the ferryboat stopped at the mouth of a woody\r\nhollow and tied up. The crowd swarmed ashore and soon the forest\r\ndistances and craggy heights echoed far and near with shoutings and\r\nlaughter. All the different ways of getting hot and tired were gone\r\nthrough with, and by-and-by the rovers straggled back to camp fortified\r\nwith responsible appetites, and then the destruction of the good things\r\nbegan. After the feast there was a refreshing season of rest and chat in\r\nthe shade of spreading oaks. By-and-by somebody shouted:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s ready for the cave?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverybody was. Bundles of candles were procured, and straightway there\r\nwas a general scamper up the hill. The mouth of the cave was up the\r\nhillside--an opening shaped like a letter A. Its massive oaken door stood\r\nunbarred. Within was a small chamber, chilly as an icehouse, and walled\r\nby Nature with solid limestone that was dewy with a cold sweat. It was\r\nromantic and mysterious to stand here in the deep gloom and look out\r\nupon the green valley shining in the sun. But the impressiveness of the\r\nsituation quickly wore off, and the romping began again. The moment\r\na candle was lighted there was a general rush upon the owner of it; a\r\nstruggle and a gallant defence followed, but the candle was soon knocked\r\ndown or blown out, and then there was a glad clamor of laughter and a\r\nnew chase. But all things have an end. By-and-by the procession went\r\nfiling down the steep descent of the main avenue, the flickering rank of\r\nlights dimly revealing the lofty walls of rock almost to their point of\r\njunction sixty feet overhead. This main avenue was not more than\r\neight or ten feet wide. Every few steps other lofty and still narrower\r\ncrevices branched from it on either hand--for McDougal\xe2\x80\x99s cave was but a\r\nvast labyrinth of crooked aisles that ran into each other and out again\r\nand led nowhere. It was said that one might wander days and nights\r\ntogether through its intricate tangle of rifts and chasms, and never\r\nfind the end of the cave; and that he might go down, and down, and\r\nstill down, into the earth, and it was just the same--labyrinth under\r\nlabyrinth, and no end to any of them. No man \xe2\x80\x9cknew\xe2\x80\x9d the cave. That was\r\nan impossible thing. Most of the young men knew a portion of it, and it\r\nwas not customary to venture much beyond this known portion. Tom Sawyer\r\nknew as much of the cave as any one.\r\n\r\nThe procession moved along the main avenue some three-quarters of\r\na mile, and then groups and couples began to slip aside into branch\r\navenues, fly along the dismal corridors, and take each other by surprise\r\nat points where the corridors joined again. Parties were able to elude\r\neach other for the space of half an hour without going beyond the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cknown\xe2\x80\x9d ground.\r\n\r\nBy-and-by, one group after another came straggling back to the mouth\r\nof the cave, panting, hilarious, smeared from head to foot with tallow\r\ndrippings, daubed with clay, and entirely delighted with the success of\r\nthe day. Then they were astonished to find that they had been taking\r\nno note of time and that night was about at hand. The clanging bell had\r\nbeen calling for half an hour. However, this sort of close to the day\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nadventures was romantic and therefore satisfactory. When the ferryboat\r\nwith her wild freight pushed into the stream, nobody cared sixpence for\r\nthe wasted time but the captain of the craft.\r\n\r\nHuck was already upon his watch when the ferryboat\xe2\x80\x99s lights went\r\nglinting past the wharf. He heard no noise on board, for the young\r\npeople were as subdued and still as people usually are who are nearly\r\ntired to death. He wondered what boat it was, and why she did not\r\nstop at the wharf--and then he dropped her out of his mind and put his\r\nattention upon his business. The night was growing cloudy and dark. Ten\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock came, and the noise of vehicles ceased, scattered lights began\r\nto wink out, all straggling foot-passengers disappeared, the village\r\nbetook itself to its slumbers and left the small watcher alone with the\r\nsilence and the ghosts. Eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock came, and the tavern lights were\r\nput out; darkness everywhere, now. Huck waited what seemed a weary long\r\ntime, but nothing happened. His faith was weakening. Was there any use?\r\nWas there really any use? Why not give it up and turn in?\r\n\r\nA noise fell upon his ear. He was all attention in an instant. The alley\r\ndoor closed softly. He sprang to the corner of the brick store. The next\r\nmoment two men brushed by him, and one seemed to have something under\r\nhis arm. It must be that box! So they were going to remove the treasure.\r\nWhy call Tom now? It would be absurd--the men would get away with the box\r\nand never be found again. No, he would stick to their wake and follow\r\nthem; he would trust to the darkness for security from discovery. So\r\ncommuning with himself, Huck stepped out and glided along behind the\r\nmen, cat-like, with bare feet, allowing them to keep just far enough\r\nahead not to be invisible.\r\n\r\nThey moved up the river street three blocks, then turned to the left up\r\na crossstreet. They went straight ahead, then, until they came to the\r\npath that led up Cardiff Hill; this they took. They passed by the old\r\nWelshman\xe2\x80\x99s house, halfway up the hill, without hesitating, and still\r\nclimbed upward. Good, thought Huck, they will bury it in the old quarry.\r\nBut they never stopped at the quarry. They passed on, up the summit.\r\nThey plunged into the narrow path between the tall sumach bushes, and\r\nwere at once hidden in the gloom. Huck closed up and shortened his\r\ndistance, now, for they would never be able to see him. He trotted along\r\nawhile; then slackened his pace, fearing he was gaining too fast; moved\r\non a piece, then stopped altogether; listened; no sound; none, save that\r\nhe seemed to hear the beating of his own heart. The hooting of an\r\nowl came over the hill--ominous sound! But no footsteps. Heavens, was\r\neverything lost! He was about to spring with winged feet, when a man\r\ncleared his throat not four feet from him! Huck\xe2\x80\x99s heart shot into his\r\nthroat, but he swallowed it again; and then he stood there shaking as\r\nif a dozen agues had taken charge of him at once, and so weak that he\r\nthought he must surely fall to the ground. He knew where he was. He\r\nknew he was within five steps of the stile leading into Widow Douglas\xe2\x80\x99\r\ngrounds. Very well, he thought, let them bury it there; it won\xe2\x80\x99t be hard\r\nto find.\r\n\r\nNow there was a voice--a very low voice--Injun Joe\xe2\x80\x99s:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDamn her, maybe she\xe2\x80\x99s got company--there\xe2\x80\x99s lights, late as it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t see any.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was that stranger\xe2\x80\x99s voice--the stranger of the haunted house. A\r\ndeadly chill went to Huck\xe2\x80\x99s heart--this, then, was the \xe2\x80\x9crevenge\xe2\x80\x9d job! His\r\nthought was, to fly. Then he remembered that the Widow Douglas had been\r\nkind to him more than once, and maybe these men were going to murder\r\nher. He wished he dared venture to warn her; but he knew he didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndare--they might come and catch him. He thought all this and more in\r\nthe moment that elapsed between the stranger\xe2\x80\x99s remark and Injun Joe\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnext--which was--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause the bush is in your way. Now--this way--now you see, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Well, there _is_ company there, I reckon. Better give it up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive it up, and I just leaving this country forever! Give it up and\r\nmaybe never have another chance. I tell you again, as I\xe2\x80\x99ve told you\r\nbefore, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for her swag--you may have it. But her husband was\r\nrough on me--many times he was rough on me--and mainly he was the justice\r\nof the peace that jugged me for a vagrant. And that ain\xe2\x80\x99t all. It ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\na millionth part of it! He had me _horsewhipped_!--horsewhipped in\r\nfront of the jail, like a nigger!--with all the town looking on!\r\n_Horsewhipped_!--do you understand? He took advantage of me and died. But\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll take it out of _her_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t kill her! Don\xe2\x80\x99t do that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKill? Who said anything about killing? I would kill _him_ if he was\r\nhere; but not her. When you want to get revenge on a woman you don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nkill her--bosh! you go for her looks. You slit her nostrils--you notch her\r\nears like a sow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy God, that\xe2\x80\x99s--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep your opinion to yourself! It will be safest for you. I\xe2\x80\x99ll tie her\r\nto the bed. If she bleeds to death, is that my fault? I\xe2\x80\x99ll not cry, if\r\nshe does. My friend, you\xe2\x80\x99ll help me in this thing--for _my_ sake--that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhy you\xe2\x80\x99re here--I mightn\xe2\x80\x99t be able alone. If you flinch, I\xe2\x80\x99ll kill you.\r\nDo you understand that? And if I have to kill you, I\xe2\x80\x99ll kill her--and\r\nthen I reckon nobody\xe2\x80\x99ll ever know much about who done this business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if it\xe2\x80\x99s got to be done, let\xe2\x80\x99s get at it. The quicker the\r\nbetter--I\xe2\x80\x99m all in a shiver.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo it _now_? And company there? Look here--I\xe2\x80\x99ll get suspicious of you,\r\nfirst thing you know. No--we\xe2\x80\x99ll wait till the lights are out--there\xe2\x80\x99s no\r\nhurry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck felt that a silence was going to ensue--a thing still more awful\r\nthan any amount of murderous talk; so he held his breath and stepped\r\ngingerly back; planted his foot carefully and firmly, after balancing,\r\none-legged, in a precarious way and almost toppling over, first on one\r\nside and then on the other. He took another step back, with the same\r\nelaboration and the same risks; then another and another, and--a twig\r\nsnapped under his foot! His breath stopped and he listened. There was no\r\nsound--the stillness was perfect. His gratitude was measureless. Now he\r\nturned in his tracks, between the walls of sumach bushes--turned\r\nhimself as carefully as if he were a ship--and then stepped quickly but\r\ncautiously along. When he emerged at the quarry he felt secure, and\r\nso he picked up his nimble heels and flew. Down, down he sped, till he\r\nreached the Welshman\xe2\x80\x99s. He banged at the door, and presently the heads\r\nof the old man and his two stalwart sons were thrust from windows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the row there? Who\xe2\x80\x99s banging? What do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me in--quick! I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, who are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuckleberry Finn--quick, let me in!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuckleberry Finn, indeed! It ain\xe2\x80\x99t a name to open many doors, I judge!\r\nBut let him in, lads, and let\xe2\x80\x99s see what\xe2\x80\x99s the trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t ever tell I told you,\xe2\x80\x9d were Huck\xe2\x80\x99s first words when he got\r\nin. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t--I\xe2\x80\x99d be killed, sure--but the widow\xe2\x80\x99s been good friends\r\nto me sometimes, and I want to tell--I _will_ tell if you\xe2\x80\x99ll promise you\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t ever say it was me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy George, he _has_ got something to tell, or he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t act so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n exclaimed the old man; \xe2\x80\x9cout with it and nobody here\xe2\x80\x99ll ever tell, lad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThree minutes later the old man and his sons, well armed, were up the\r\nhill, and just entering the sumach path on tiptoe, their weapons in\r\ntheir hands. Huck accompanied them no further. He hid behind a great\r\nbowlder and fell to listening. There was a lagging, anxious silence, and\r\nthen all of a sudden there was an explosion of firearms and a cry.\r\n\r\nHuck waited for no particulars. He sprang away and sped down the hill as\r\nfast as his legs could carry him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXX\r\n\r\nAS the earliest suspicion of dawn appeared on Sunday morning, Huck came\r\ngroping up the hill and rapped gently at the old Welshman\xe2\x80\x99s door. The\r\ninmates were asleep, but it was a sleep that was set on a hair-trigger,\r\non account of the exciting episode of the night. A call came from a\r\nwindow:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s there!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck\xe2\x80\x99s scared voice answered in a low tone:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease let me in! It\xe2\x80\x99s only Huck Finn!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a name that can open this door night or day, lad!--and welcome!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese were strange words to the vagabond boy\xe2\x80\x99s ears, and the pleasantest\r\nhe had ever heard. He could not recollect that the closing word had ever\r\nbeen applied in his case before. The door was quickly unlocked, and he\r\nentered. Huck was given a seat and the old man and his brace of tall\r\nsons speedily dressed themselves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, my boy, I hope you\xe2\x80\x99re good and hungry, because breakfast will be\r\nready as soon as the sun\xe2\x80\x99s up, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll have a piping hot one, too--make\r\nyourself easy about that! I and the boys hoped you\xe2\x80\x99d turn up and stop\r\nhere last night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was awful scared,\xe2\x80\x9d said Huck, \xe2\x80\x9cand I run. I took out when the pistols\r\nwent off, and I didn\xe2\x80\x99t stop for three mile. I\xe2\x80\x99ve come now becuz I wanted\r\nto know about it, you know; and I come before daylight becuz I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwant to run across them devils, even if they was dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, poor chap, you do look as if you\xe2\x80\x99d had a hard night of it--but\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s a bed here for you when you\xe2\x80\x99ve had your breakfast. No, they\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t dead, lad--we are sorry enough for that. You see we knew right\r\nwhere to put our hands on them, by your description; so we crept along\r\non tiptoe till we got within fifteen feet of them--dark as a cellar that\r\nsumach path was--and just then I found I was going to sneeze. It was the\r\nmeanest kind of luck! I tried to keep it back, but no use--\xe2\x80\x99twas bound to\r\ncome, and it did come! I was in the lead with my pistol raised, and when\r\nthe sneeze started those scoundrels a-rustling to get out of the path,\r\nI sung out, \xe2\x80\x98Fire boys!\xe2\x80\x99 and blazed away at the place where the rustling\r\nwas. So did the boys. But they were off in a jiffy, those villains, and\r\nwe after them, down through the woods. I judge we never touched them.\r\nThey fired a shot apiece as they started, but their bullets whizzed by\r\nand didn\xe2\x80\x99t do us any harm. As soon as we lost the sound of their feet\r\nwe quit chasing, and went down and stirred up the constables. They got a\r\nposse together, and went off to guard the river bank, and as soon as it\r\nis light the sheriff and a gang are going to beat up the woods. My boys\r\nwill be with them presently. I wish we had some sort of description of\r\nthose rascals--\xe2\x80\x99twould help a good deal. But you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see what they\r\nwere like, in the dark, lad, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes; I saw them downtown and follered them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSplendid! Describe them--describe them, my boy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne\xe2\x80\x99s the old deaf and dumb Spaniard that\xe2\x80\x99s ben around here once or\r\ntwice, and t\xe2\x80\x99other\xe2\x80\x99s a mean-looking, ragged--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s enough, lad, we know the men! Happened on them in the woods back\r\nof the widow\xe2\x80\x99s one day, and they slunk away. Off with you, boys, and\r\ntell the sheriff--get your breakfast tomorrow morning!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Welshman\xe2\x80\x99s sons departed at once. As they were leaving the room Huck\r\nsprang up and exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, please don\xe2\x80\x99t tell _any_body it was me that blowed on them! Oh,\r\nplease!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right if you say it, Huck, but you ought to have the credit of what\r\nyou did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, no! Please don\xe2\x80\x99t tell!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the young men were gone, the old Welshman said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey won\xe2\x80\x99t tell--and I won\xe2\x80\x99t. But why don\xe2\x80\x99t you want it known?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck would not explain, further than to say that he already knew too\r\nmuch about one of those men and would not have the man know that he knew\r\nanything against him for the whole world--he would be killed for knowing\r\nit, sure.\r\n\r\nThe old man promised secrecy once more, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you come to follow these fellows, lad? Were they looking\r\nsuspicious?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck was silent while he framed a duly cautious reply. Then he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you see, I\xe2\x80\x99m a kind of a hard lot,--least everybody says so, and\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t see nothing agin it--and sometimes I can\xe2\x80\x99t sleep much, on account\r\nof thinking about it and sort of trying to strike out a new way of\r\ndoing. That was the way of it last night. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t sleep, and so I\r\ncome along upstreet \xe2\x80\x99bout midnight, a-turning it all over, and when I\r\ngot to that old shackly brick store by the Temperance Tavern, I backed\r\nup agin the wall to have another think. Well, just then along comes\r\nthese two chaps slipping along close by me, with something under their\r\narm, and I reckoned they\xe2\x80\x99d stole it. One was a-smoking, and t\xe2\x80\x99other one\r\nwanted a light; so they stopped right before me and the cigars lit up\r\ntheir faces and I see that the big one was the deaf and dumb Spaniard,\r\nby his white whiskers and the patch on his eye, and t\xe2\x80\x99other one was a\r\nrusty, ragged-looking devil.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould you see the rags by the light of the cigars?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis staggered Huck for a moment. Then he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know--but somehow it seems as if I did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen they went on, and you--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFollered \xe2\x80\x99em--yes. That was it. I wanted to see what was up--they sneaked\r\nalong so. I dogged \xe2\x80\x99em to the widder\xe2\x80\x99s stile, and stood in the dark and\r\nheard the ragged one beg for the widder, and the Spaniard swear he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nspile her looks just as I told you and your two--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! The _deaf and dumb_ man said all that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck had made another terrible mistake! He was trying his best to keep\r\nthe old man from getting the faintest hint of who the Spaniard might be,\r\nand yet his tongue seemed determined to get him into trouble in spite of\r\nall he could do. He made several efforts to creep out of his scrape,\r\nbut the old man\xe2\x80\x99s eye was upon him and he made blunder after blunder.\r\nPresently the Welshman said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy boy, don\xe2\x80\x99t be afraid of me. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t hurt a hair of your head for\r\nall the world. No--I\xe2\x80\x99d protect you--I\xe2\x80\x99d protect you. This Spaniard is\r\nnot deaf and dumb; you\xe2\x80\x99ve let that slip without intending it; you can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncover that up now. You know something about that Spaniard that you want\r\nto keep dark. Now trust me--tell me what it is, and trust me--I won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbetray you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck looked into the old man\xe2\x80\x99s honest eyes a moment, then bent over and\r\nwhispered in his ear:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tain\xe2\x80\x99t a Spaniard--it\xe2\x80\x99s Injun Joe!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Welshman almost jumped out of his chair. In a moment he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all plain enough, now. When you talked about notching ears and\r\nslitting noses I judged that that was your own embellishment, because\r\nwhite men don\xe2\x80\x99t take that sort of revenge. But an Injun! That\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\ndifferent matter altogether.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDuring breakfast the talk went on, and in the course of it the old man\r\nsaid that the last thing which he and his sons had done, before going\r\nto bed, was to get a lantern and examine the stile and its vicinity for\r\nmarks of blood. They found none, but captured a bulky bundle of--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf _what_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIf the words had been lightning they could not have leaped with a more\r\nstunning suddenness from Huck\xe2\x80\x99s blanched lips. His eyes were staring\r\nwide, now, and his breath suspended--waiting for the answer. The Welshman\r\nstarted--stared in return--three seconds--five seconds--ten--then replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf burglar\xe2\x80\x99s tools. Why, what\xe2\x80\x99s the _matter_ with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck sank back, panting gently, but deeply, unutterably grateful. The\r\nWelshman eyed him gravely, curiously--and presently said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, burglar\xe2\x80\x99s tools. That appears to relieve you a good deal. But what\r\ndid give you that turn? What were _you_ expecting we\xe2\x80\x99d found?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck was in a close place--the inquiring eye was upon him--he would have\r\ngiven anything for material for a plausible answer--nothing suggested\r\nitself--the inquiring eye was boring deeper and deeper--a senseless\r\nreply offered--there was no time to weigh it, so at a venture he uttered\r\nit--feebly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSunday-school books, maybe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoor Huck was too distressed to smile, but the old man laughed loud and\r\njoyously, shook up the details of his anatomy from head to foot, and\r\nended by saying that such a laugh was money in a-man\xe2\x80\x99s pocket, because\r\nit cut down the doctor\xe2\x80\x99s bill like everything. Then he added:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor old chap, you\xe2\x80\x99re white and jaded--you ain\xe2\x80\x99t well a bit--no wonder\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re a little flighty and off your balance. But you\xe2\x80\x99ll come out of it.\r\nRest and sleep will fetch you out all right, I hope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck was irritated to think he had been such a goose and betrayed such\r\na suspicious excitement, for he had dropped the idea that the parcel\r\nbrought from the tavern was the treasure, as soon as he had heard the\r\ntalk at the widow\xe2\x80\x99s stile. He had only thought it was not the treasure,\r\nhowever--he had not known that it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t--and so the suggestion of a\r\ncaptured bundle was too much for his self-possession. But on the whole\r\nhe felt glad the little episode had happened, for now he knew beyond all\r\nquestion that that bundle was not _the_ bundle, and so his mind was\r\nat rest and exceedingly comfortable. In fact, everything seemed to be\r\ndrifting just in the right direction, now; the treasure must be still\r\nin No. 2, the men would be captured and jailed that day, and he and\r\nTom could seize the gold that night without any trouble or any fear of\r\ninterruption.\r\n\r\nJust as breakfast was completed there was a knock at the door. Huck\r\njumped for a hiding-place, for he had no mind to be connected even\r\nremotely with the late event. The Welshman admitted several ladies and\r\ngentlemen, among them the Widow Douglas, and noticed that groups of\r\ncitizens were climbing up the hill--to stare at the stile. So the news\r\nhad spread. The Welshman had to tell the story of the night to the\r\nvisitors. The widow\xe2\x80\x99s gratitude for her preservation was outspoken.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say a word about it, madam. There\xe2\x80\x99s another that you\xe2\x80\x99re more\r\nbeholden to than you are to me and my boys, maybe, but he don\xe2\x80\x99t allow me\r\nto tell his name. We wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been there but for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOf course this excited a curiosity so vast that it almost belittled the\r\nmain matter--but the Welshman allowed it to eat into the vitals of his\r\nvisitors, and through them be transmitted to the whole town, for he\r\nrefused to part with his secret. When all else had been learned, the\r\nwidow said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI went to sleep reading in bed and slept straight through all that\r\nnoise. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you come and wake me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe judged it warn\xe2\x80\x99t worth while. Those fellows warn\xe2\x80\x99t likely to come\r\nagain--they hadn\xe2\x80\x99t any tools left to work with, and what was the use of\r\nwaking you up and scaring you to death? My three negro men stood guard\r\nat your house all the rest of the night. They\xe2\x80\x99ve just come back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMore visitors came, and the story had to be told and retold for a couple\r\nof hours more.\r\n\r\nThere was no Sabbath-school during day-school vacation, but everybody\r\nwas early at church. The stirring event was well canvassed. News came\r\nthat not a sign of the two villains had been yet discovered. When the\r\nsermon was finished, Judge Thatcher\xe2\x80\x99s wife dropped alongside of Mrs.\r\nHarper as she moved down the aisle with the crowd and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs my Becky going to sleep all day? I just expected she would be tired\r\nto death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour Becky?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d with a startled look--\xe2\x80\x9cdidn\xe2\x80\x99t she stay with you last night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Thatcher turned pale, and sank into a pew, just as Aunt Polly,\r\ntalking briskly with a friend, passed by. Aunt Polly said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodmorning, Mrs. Thatcher. Goodmorning, Mrs. Harper. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got a boy\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s turned up missing. I reckon my Tom stayed at your house last\r\nnight--one of you. And now he\xe2\x80\x99s afraid to come to church.
