I\xe2\x80\x99ve got to\r\nsettle with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Thatcher shook her head feebly and turned paler than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe didn\xe2\x80\x99t stay with us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Harper, beginning to look uneasy. A\r\nmarked anxiety came into Aunt Polly\xe2\x80\x99s face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJoe Harper, have you seen my Tom this morning?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x99m.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did you see him last?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJoe tried to remember, but was not sure he could say. The people had\r\nstopped moving out of church. Whispers passed along, and a boding\r\nuneasiness took possession of every countenance. Children were anxiously\r\nquestioned, and young teachers. They all said they had not noticed\r\nwhether Tom and Becky were on board the ferryboat on the homeward trip;\r\nit was dark; no one thought of inquiring if any one was missing. One\r\nyoung man finally blurted out his fear that they were still in the cave!\r\nMrs. Thatcher swooned away. Aunt Polly fell to crying and wringing her\r\nhands.\r\n\r\nThe alarm swept from lip to lip, from group to group, from street to\r\nstreet, and within five minutes the bells were wildly clanging and\r\nthe whole town was up! The Cardiff Hill episode sank into instant\r\ninsignificance, the burglars were forgotten, horses were saddled, skiffs\r\nwere manned, the ferryboat ordered out, and before the horror was half\r\nan hour old, two hundred men were pouring down highroad and river toward\r\nthe cave.\r\n\r\nAll the long afternoon the village seemed empty and dead. Many women\r\nvisited Aunt Polly and Mrs. Thatcher and tried to comfort them. They\r\ncried with them, too, and that was still better than words. All the\r\ntedious night the town waited for news; but when the morning dawned at\r\nlast, all the word that came was, \xe2\x80\x9cSend more candles--and send food.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Mrs. Thatcher was almost crazed; and Aunt Polly, also. Judge Thatcher\r\nsent messages of hope and encouragement from the cave, but they conveyed\r\nno real cheer.\r\n\r\nThe old Welshman came home toward daylight, spattered with\r\ncandle-grease, smeared with clay, and almost worn out. He found Huck\r\nstill in the bed that had been provided for him, and delirious with\r\nfever. The physicians were all at the cave, so the Widow Douglas came\r\nand took charge of the patient. She said she would do her best by him,\r\nbecause, whether he was good, bad, or indifferent, he was the Lord\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nand nothing that was the Lord\xe2\x80\x99s was a thing to be neglected. The\r\nWelshman said Huck had good spots in him, and the widow said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can depend on it. That\xe2\x80\x99s the Lord\xe2\x80\x99s mark. He don\xe2\x80\x99t leave it off.\r\nHe never does. Puts it somewhere on every creature that comes from his\r\nhands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEarly in the forenoon parties of jaded men began to straggle into the\r\nvillage, but the strongest of the citizens continued searching. All the\r\nnews that could be gained was that remotenesses of the cavern were being\r\nransacked that had never been visited before; that every corner and\r\ncrevice was going to be thoroughly searched; that wherever one wandered\r\nthrough the maze of passages, lights were to be seen flitting hither\r\nand thither in the distance, and shoutings and pistol-shots sent their\r\nhollow reverberations to the ear down the sombre aisles. In one place,\r\nfar from the section usually traversed by tourists, the names \xe2\x80\x9cBECKY &\r\nTOM\xe2\x80\x9d had been found traced upon the rocky wall with candle-smoke, and\r\nnear at hand a grease-soiled bit of ribbon. Mrs. Thatcher recognized the\r\nribbon and cried over it. She said it was the last relic she should ever\r\nhave of her child; and that no other memorial of her could ever be so\r\nprecious, because this one parted latest from the living body before the\r\nawful death came. Some said that now and then, in the cave, a far-away\r\nspeck of light would glimmer, and then a glorious shout would burst\r\nforth and a score of men go trooping down the echoing aisle--and then a\r\nsickening disappointment always followed; the children were not there;\r\nit was only a searcher\xe2\x80\x99s light.\r\n\r\nThree dreadful days and nights dragged their tedious hours along, and\r\nthe village sank into a hopeless stupor. No one had heart for anything.\r\nThe accidental discovery, just made, that the proprietor of the\r\nTemperance Tavern kept liquor on his premises, scarcely fluttered the\r\npublic pulse, tremendous as the fact was. In a lucid interval, Huck\r\nfeebly led up to the subject of taverns, and finally asked--dimly\r\ndreading the worst--if anything had been discovered at the Temperance\r\nTavern since he had been ill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the widow.\r\n\r\nHuck started up in bed, wildeyed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat? What was it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLiquor!--and the place has been shut up. Lie down, child--what a turn you\r\ndid give me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly tell me just one thing--only just one--please! Was it Tom Sawyer\r\nthat found it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe widow burst into tears. \xe2\x80\x9cHush, hush, child, hush! I\xe2\x80\x99ve told you\r\nbefore, you must _not_ talk. You are very, very sick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen nothing but liquor had been found; there would have been a great\r\npowwow if it had been the gold. So the treasure was gone forever--gone\r\nforever! But what could she be crying about? Curious that she should\r\ncry.\r\n\r\nThese thoughts worked their dim way through Huck\xe2\x80\x99s mind, and under the\r\nweariness they gave him he fell asleep. The widow said to herself:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere--he\xe2\x80\x99s asleep, poor wreck. Tom Sawyer find it! Pity but somebody\r\ncould find Tom Sawyer! Ah, there ain\xe2\x80\x99t many left, now, that\xe2\x80\x99s got hope\r\nenough, or strength enough, either, to go on searching.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXI\r\n\r\nNOW to return to Tom and Becky\xe2\x80\x99s share in the picnic. They tripped along\r\nthe murky aisles with the rest of the company, visiting the familiar\r\nwonders of the cave--wonders dubbed with rather over-descriptive names,\r\nsuch as \xe2\x80\x9cThe Drawing-Room,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cThe Cathedral,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cAladdin\xe2\x80\x99s Palace,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nso on. Presently the hide-and-seek frolicking began, and Tom and Becky\r\nengaged in it with zeal until the exertion began to grow a trifle\r\nwearisome; then they wandered down a sinuous avenue holding their\r\ncandles aloft and reading the tangled webwork of names, dates,\r\npostoffice addresses, and mottoes with which the rocky walls had been\r\nfrescoed (in candle-smoke). Still drifting along and talking, they\r\nscarcely noticed that they were now in a part of the cave whose walls\r\nwere not frescoed. They smoked their own names under an overhanging\r\nshelf and moved on. Presently they came to a place where a little stream\r\nof water, trickling over a ledge and carrying a limestone sediment with\r\nit, had, in the slow-dragging ages, formed a laced and ruffled Niagara\r\nin gleaming and imperishable stone. Tom squeezed his small body behind\r\nit in order to illuminate it for Becky\xe2\x80\x99s gratification. He found that\r\nit curtained a sort of steep natural stairway which was enclosed between\r\nnarrow walls, and at once the ambition to be a discoverer seized him.\r\n\r\nBecky responded to his call, and they made a smoke-mark for future\r\nguidance, and started upon their quest. They wound this way and that,\r\nfar down into the secret depths of the cave, made another mark, and\r\nbranched off in search of novelties to tell the upper world about. In\r\none place they found a spacious cavern, from whose ceiling depended a\r\nmultitude of shining stalactites of the length and circumference of\r\na man\xe2\x80\x99s leg; they walked all about it, wondering and admiring, and\r\npresently left it by one of the numerous passages that opened into\r\nit. This shortly brought them to a bewitching spring, whose basin was\r\nincrusted with a frostwork of glittering crystals; it was in the midst\r\nof a cavern whose walls were supported by many fantastic pillars which\r\nhad been formed by the joining of great stalactites and stalagmites\r\ntogether, the result of the ceaseless water-drip of centuries. Under the\r\nroof vast knots of bats had packed themselves together, thousands in a\r\nbunch; the lights disturbed the creatures and they came flocking down by\r\nhundreds, squeaking and darting furiously at the candles. Tom knew their\r\nways and the danger of this sort of conduct. He seized Becky\xe2\x80\x99s hand and\r\nhurried her into the first corridor that offered; and none too soon, for\r\na bat struck Becky\xe2\x80\x99s light out with its wing while she was passing out\r\nof the cavern. The bats chased the children a good distance; but the\r\nfugitives plunged into every new passage that offered, and at last got\r\nrid of the perilous things. Tom found a subterranean lake, shortly,\r\nwhich stretched its dim length away until its shape was lost in the\r\nshadows. He wanted to explore its borders, but concluded that it would\r\nbe best to sit down and rest awhile, first. Now, for the first time, the\r\ndeep stillness of the place laid a clammy hand upon the spirits of the\r\nchildren. Becky said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t notice, but it seems ever so long since I heard any of\r\nthe others.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome to think, Becky, we are away down below them--and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how\r\nfar away north, or south, or east, or whichever it is. We couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hear\r\nthem here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBecky grew apprehensive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder how long we\xe2\x80\x99ve been down here, Tom? We better start back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I reckon we better. P\xe2\x80\x99raps we better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you find the way, Tom? It\xe2\x80\x99s all a mixed-up crookedness to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon I could find it--but then the bats. If they put our candles\r\nout it will be an awful fix. Let\xe2\x80\x99s try some other way, so as not to go\r\nthrough there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell. But I hope we won\xe2\x80\x99t get lost. It would be so awful!\xe2\x80\x9d and the girl\r\nshuddered at the thought of the dreadful possibilities.\r\n\r\nThey started through a corridor, and traversed it in silence a long\r\nway, glancing at each new opening, to see if there was anything familiar\r\nabout the look of it; but they were all strange. Every time Tom made an\r\nexamination, Becky would watch his face for an encouraging sign, and he\r\nwould say cheerily:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s all right. This ain\xe2\x80\x99t the one, but we\xe2\x80\x99ll come to it right\r\naway!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut he felt less and less hopeful with each failure, and presently began\r\nto turn off into diverging avenues at sheer random, in desperate hope of\r\nfinding the one that was wanted. He still said it was \xe2\x80\x9call right,\xe2\x80\x9d but\r\nthere was such a leaden dread at his heart that the words had lost their\r\nring and sounded just as if he had said, \xe2\x80\x9cAll is lost!\xe2\x80\x9d Becky clung to\r\nhis side in an anguish of fear, and tried hard to keep back the tears,\r\nbut they would come. At last she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Tom, never mind the bats, let\xe2\x80\x99s go back that way! We seem to get\r\nworse and worse off all the time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen!\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\nProfound silence; silence so deep that even their breathings were\r\nconspicuous in the hush. Tom shouted. The call went echoing down\r\nthe empty aisles and died out in the distance in a faint sound that\r\nresembled a ripple of mocking laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t do it again, Tom, it is too horrid,\xe2\x80\x9d said Becky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is horrid, but I better, Becky; they might hear us, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nhe shouted again.\r\n\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x9cmight\xe2\x80\x9d was even a chillier horror than the ghostly laughter, it so\r\nconfessed a perishing hope. The children stood still and listened; but\r\nthere was no result. Tom turned upon the back track at once, and hurried\r\nhis steps. It was but a little while before a certain indecision in his\r\nmanner revealed another fearful fact to Becky--he could not find his way\r\nback!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Tom, you didn\xe2\x80\x99t make any marks!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecky, I was such a fool! Such a fool! I never thought we might want to\r\ncome back! No--I can\xe2\x80\x99t find the way. It\xe2\x80\x99s all mixed up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, Tom, we\xe2\x80\x99re lost! we\xe2\x80\x99re lost! We never can get out of this awful\r\nplace! Oh, why _did_ we ever leave the others!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe sank to the ground and burst into such a frenzy of crying that Tom\r\nwas appalled with the idea that she might die, or lose her reason. He\r\nsat down by her and put his arms around her; she buried her face in\r\nhis bosom, she clung to him, she poured out her terrors, her unavailing\r\nregrets, and the far echoes turned them all to jeering laughter. Tom\r\nbegged her to pluck up hope again, and she said she could not. He fell\r\nto blaming and abusing himself for getting her into this miserable\r\nsituation; this had a better effect. She said she would try to hope\r\nagain, she would get up and follow wherever he might lead if only he\r\nwould not talk like that any more. For he was no more to blame than she,\r\nshe said.\r\n\r\nSo they moved on again--aimlessly--simply at random--all they could do\r\nwas to move, keep moving. For a little while, hope made a show of\r\nreviving--not with any reason to back it, but only because it is its\r\nnature to revive when the spring has not been taken out of it by age and\r\nfamiliarity with failure.\r\n\r\nBy-and-by Tom took Becky\xe2\x80\x99s candle and blew it out. This economy meant so\r\nmuch! Words were not needed. Becky understood, and her hope died again.\r\nShe knew that Tom had a whole candle and three or four pieces in his\r\npockets--yet he must economize.\r\n\r\nBy-and-by, fatigue began to assert its claims; the children tried to pay\r\nattention, for it was dreadful to think of sitting down when time was\r\ngrown to be so precious, moving, in some direction, in any direction,\r\nwas at least progress and might bear fruit; but to sit down was to\r\ninvite death and shorten its pursuit.\r\n\r\nAt last Becky\xe2\x80\x99s frail limbs refused to carry her farther. She sat down.\r\nTom rested with her, and they talked of home, and the friends there,\r\nand the comfortable beds and, above all, the light! Becky cried, and Tom\r\ntried to think of some way of comforting her, but all his encouragements\r\nwere grown thread-bare with use, and sounded like sarcasms. Fatigue bore\r\nso heavily upon Becky that she drowsed off to sleep. Tom was grateful.\r\nHe sat looking into her drawn face and saw it grow smooth and natural\r\nunder the influence of pleasant dreams; and by-and-by a smile dawned and\r\nrested there. The peaceful face reflected somewhat of peace and healing\r\ninto his own spirit, and his thoughts wandered away to bygone times and\r\ndreamy memories. While he was deep in his musings, Becky woke up with a\r\nbreezy little laugh--but it was stricken dead upon her lips, and a groan\r\nfollowed it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, how _could_ I sleep! I wish I never, never had waked! No! No, I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t, Tom! Don\xe2\x80\x99t look so! I won\xe2\x80\x99t say it again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad you\xe2\x80\x99ve slept, Becky; you\xe2\x80\x99ll feel rested, now, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll find\r\nthe way out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can try, Tom; but I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen such a beautiful country in my dream. I\r\nreckon we are going there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe not, maybe not. Cheer up, Becky, and let\xe2\x80\x99s go on trying.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey rose up and wandered along, hand in hand and hopeless. They tried\r\nto estimate how long they had been in the cave, but all they knew was\r\nthat it seemed days and weeks, and yet it was plain that this could not\r\nbe, for their candles were not gone yet. A long time after this--they\r\ncould not tell how long--Tom said they must go softly and listen for\r\ndripping water--they must find a spring. They found one presently, and\r\nTom said it was time to rest again. Both were cruelly tired, yet Becky\r\nsaid she thought she could go a little farther. She was surprised to\r\nhear Tom dissent. She could not understand it. They sat down, and Tom\r\nfastened his candle to the wall in front of them with some clay. Thought\r\nwas soon busy; nothing was said for some time. Then Becky broke the\r\nsilence:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, I am so hungry!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom took something out of his pocket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you remember this?\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\nBecky almost smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s our wedding-cake, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes--I wish it was as big as a barrel, for it\xe2\x80\x99s all we\xe2\x80\x99ve got.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saved it from the picnic for us to dream on, Tom, the way grownup\r\npeople do with wedding-cake--but it\xe2\x80\x99ll be our--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe dropped the sentence where it was. Tom divided the cake and Becky\r\nate with good appetite, while Tom nibbled at his moiety. There was\r\nabundance of cold water to finish the feast with. By-and-by Becky\r\nsuggested that they move on again. Tom was silent a moment. Then he\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecky, can you bear it if I tell you something?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBecky\xe2\x80\x99s face paled, but she thought she could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, Becky, we must stay here, where there\xe2\x80\x99s water to drink.\r\nThat little piece is our last candle!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBecky gave loose to tears and wailings. Tom did what he could to comfort\r\nher, but with little effect. At length Becky said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Becky?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ll miss us and hunt for us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, they will! Certainly they will!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe they\xe2\x80\x99re hunting for us now, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I reckon maybe they are. I hope they are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen would they miss us, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen they get back to the boat, I reckon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, it might be dark then--would they notice we hadn\xe2\x80\x99t come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. But anyway, your mother would miss you as soon as they\r\ngot home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA frightened look in Becky\xe2\x80\x99s face brought Tom to his senses and he saw\r\nthat he had made a blunder. Becky was not to have gone home that night!\r\nThe children became silent and thoughtful. In a moment a new burst of\r\ngrief from Becky showed Tom that the thing in his mind had struck hers\r\nalso--that the Sabbath morning might be half spent before Mrs. Thatcher\r\ndiscovered that Becky was not at Mrs. Harper\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\nThe children fastened their eyes upon their bit of candle and watched it\r\nmelt slowly and pitilessly away; saw the half inch of wick stand alone\r\nat last; saw the feeble flame rise and fall, climb the thin column of\r\nsmoke, linger at its top a moment, and then--the horror of utter darkness\r\nreigned!\r\n\r\nHow long afterward it was that Becky came to a slow consciousness that\r\nshe was crying in Tom\xe2\x80\x99s arms, neither could tell. All that they knew\r\nwas, that after what seemed a mighty stretch of time, both awoke out of\r\na dead stupor of sleep and resumed their miseries once more. Tom said\r\nit might be Sunday, now--maybe Monday. He tried to get Becky to talk, but\r\nher sorrows were too oppressive, all her hopes were gone. Tom said that\r\nthey must have been missed long ago, and no doubt the search was going\r\non. He would shout and maybe some one would come. He tried it; but in\r\nthe darkness the distant echoes sounded so hideously that he tried it no\r\nmore.\r\n\r\nThe hours wasted away, and hunger came to torment the captives again. A\r\nportion of Tom\xe2\x80\x99s half of the cake was left; they divided and ate it. But\r\nthey seemed hungrier than before. The poor morsel of food only whetted\r\ndesire.\r\n\r\nBy-and-by Tom said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSH! Did you hear that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoth held their breath and listened. There was a sound like the\r\nfaintest, far-off shout. Instantly Tom answered it, and leading Becky by\r\nthe hand, started groping down the corridor in its direction. Presently\r\nhe listened again; again the sound was heard, and apparently a little\r\nnearer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s them!\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom; \xe2\x80\x9cthey\xe2\x80\x99re coming! Come along, Becky--we\xe2\x80\x99re all\r\nright now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe joy of the prisoners was almost overwhelming. Their speed was slow,\r\nhowever, because pitfalls were somewhat common, and had to be guarded\r\nagainst. They shortly came to one and had to stop. It might be three\r\nfeet deep, it might be a hundred--there was no passing it at any rate.\r\nTom got down on his breast and reached as far down as he could. No\r\nbottom. They must stay there and wait until the searchers came. They\r\nlistened; evidently the distant shoutings were growing more distant!\r\na moment or two more and they had gone altogether. The heart-sinking\r\nmisery of it! Tom whooped until he was hoarse, but it was of no use. He\r\ntalked hopefully to Becky; but an age of anxious waiting passed and no\r\nsounds came again.\r\n\r\nThe children groped their way back to the spring. The weary time dragged\r\non; they slept again, and awoke famished and woe-stricken. Tom believed\r\nit must be Tuesday by this time.\r\n\r\nNow an idea struck him. There were some side passages near at hand. It\r\nwould be better to explore some of these than bear the weight of the\r\nheavy time in idleness. He took a kite-line from his pocket, tied it to\r\na projection, and he and Becky started, Tom in the lead, unwinding the\r\nline as he groped along. At the end of twenty steps the corridor ended\r\nin a \xe2\x80\x9cjumping-off place.\xe2\x80\x9d Tom got down on his knees and felt below,\r\nand then as far around the corner as he could reach with his hands\r\nconveniently; he made an effort to stretch yet a little farther to the\r\nright, and at that moment, not twenty yards away, a human hand, holding\r\na candle, appeared from behind a rock! Tom lifted up a glorious shout,\r\nand instantly that hand was followed by the body it belonged to--Injun\r\nJoe\xe2\x80\x99s! Tom was paralyzed; he could not move. He was vastly gratified the\r\nnext moment, to see the \xe2\x80\x9cSpaniard\xe2\x80\x9d take to his heels and get himself out\r\nof sight. Tom wondered that Joe had not recognized his voice and come\r\nover and killed him for testifying in court. But the echoes must have\r\ndisguised the voice. Without doubt, that was it, he reasoned. Tom\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfright weakened every muscle in his body. He said to himself that if he\r\nhad strength enough to get back to the spring he would stay there, and\r\nnothing should tempt him to run the risk of meeting Injun Joe again. He\r\nwas careful to keep from Becky what it was he had seen. He told her he\r\nhad only shouted \xe2\x80\x9cfor luck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut hunger and wretchedness rise superior to fears in the long run.\r\nAnother tedious wait at the spring and another long sleep brought\r\nchanges. The children awoke tortured with a raging hunger. Tom believed\r\nthat it must be Wednesday or Thursday or even Friday or Saturday, now,\r\nand that the search had been given over. He proposed to explore another\r\npassage. He felt willing to risk Injun Joe and all other terrors. But\r\nBecky was very weak. She had sunk into a dreary apathy and would not be\r\nroused. She said she would wait, now, where she was, and die--it would\r\nnot be long. She told Tom to go with the kite-line and explore if he\r\nchose; but she implored him to come back every little while and speak\r\nto her; and she made him promise that when the awful time came, he would\r\nstay by her and hold her hand until all was over.\r\n\r\nTom kissed her, with a choking sensation in his throat, and made a show\r\nof being confident of finding the searchers or an escape from the cave;\r\nthen he took the kite-line in his hand and went groping down one of the\r\npassages on his hands and knees, distressed with hunger and sick with\r\nbodings of coming doom.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXII\r\n\r\nTUESDAY afternoon came, and waned to the twilight. The village of St.\r\nPetersburg still mourned. The lost children had not been found. Public\r\nprayers had been offered up for them, and many and many a private prayer\r\nthat had the petitioner\xe2\x80\x99s whole heart in it; but still no good news came\r\nfrom the cave. The majority of the searchers had given up the quest\r\nand gone back to their daily avocations, saying that it was plain the\r\nchildren could never be found. Mrs. Thatcher was very ill, and a great\r\npart of the time delirious. People said it was heartbreaking to hear her\r\ncall her child, and raise her head and listen a whole minute at a time,\r\nthen lay it wearily down again with a moan. Aunt Polly had drooped into\r\na settled melancholy, and her gray hair had grown almost white. The\r\nvillage went to its rest on Tuesday night, sad and forlorn.\r\n\r\nAway in the middle of the night a wild peal burst from the village\r\nbells, and in a moment the streets were swarming with frantic half-clad\r\npeople, who shouted, \xe2\x80\x9cTurn out! turn out! they\xe2\x80\x99re found! they\xe2\x80\x99re found!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Tin pans and horns were added to the din, the population massed itself\r\nand moved toward the river, met the children coming in an open carriage\r\ndrawn by shouting citizens, thronged around it, joined its homeward\r\nmarch, and swept magnificently up the main street roaring huzzah after\r\nhuzzah!\r\n\r\nThe village was illuminated; nobody went to bed again; it was the\r\ngreatest night the little town had ever seen. During the first half-hour\r\na procession of villagers filed through Judge Thatcher\xe2\x80\x99s house, seized\r\nthe saved ones and kissed them, squeezed Mrs. Thatcher\xe2\x80\x99s hand, tried to\r\nspeak but couldn\xe2\x80\x99t--and drifted out raining tears all over the place.\r\n\r\nAunt Polly\xe2\x80\x99s happiness was complete, and Mrs. Thatcher\xe2\x80\x99s nearly so. It\r\nwould be complete, however, as soon as the messenger dispatched with the\r\ngreat news to the cave should get the word to her husband. Tom lay upon\r\na sofa with an eager auditory about him and told the history of the\r\nwonderful adventure, putting in many striking additions to adorn it\r\nwithal; and closed with a description of how he left Becky and went\r\non an exploring expedition; how he followed two avenues as far as his\r\nkite-line would reach; how he followed a third to the fullest stretch\r\nof the kite-line, and was about to turn back when he glimpsed a far-off\r\nspeck that looked like daylight; dropped the line and groped toward it,\r\npushed his head and shoulders through a small hole, and saw the broad\r\nMississippi rolling by!\r\n\r\nAnd if it had only happened to be night he would not have seen that\r\nspeck of daylight and would not have explored that passage any more! He\r\ntold how he went back for Becky and broke the good news and she told\r\nhim not to fret her with such stuff, for she was tired, and knew she was\r\ngoing to die, and wanted to. He described how he labored with her and\r\nconvinced her; and how she almost died for joy when she had groped to\r\nwhere she actually saw the blue speck of daylight; how he pushed his way\r\nout at the hole and then helped her out; how they sat there and cried\r\nfor gladness; how some men came along in a skiff and Tom hailed them\r\nand told them their situation and their famished condition; how the men\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t believe the wild tale at first, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause,\xe2\x80\x9d said they, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are\r\nfive miles down the river below the valley the cave is in\xe2\x80\x9d--then took\r\nthem aboard, rowed to a house, gave them supper, made them rest till two\r\nor three hours after dark and then brought them home.\r\n\r\nBefore day-dawn, Judge Thatcher and the handful of searchers with him\r\nwere tracked out, in the cave, by the twine clews they had strung behind\r\nthem, and informed of the great news.\r\n\r\nThree days and nights of toil and hunger in the cave were not to\r\nbe shaken off at once, as Tom and Becky soon discovered. They were\r\nbedridden all of Wednesday and Thursday, and seemed to grow more and\r\nmore tired and worn, all the time. Tom got about, a little, on Thursday,\r\nwas downtown Friday, and nearly as whole as ever Saturday; but Becky\r\ndid not leave her room until Sunday, and then she looked as if she had\r\npassed through a wasting illness.\r\n\r\nTom learned of Huck\xe2\x80\x99s sickness and went to see him on Friday, but could\r\nnot be admitted to the bedroom; neither could he on Saturday or Sunday.\r\nHe was admitted daily after that, but was warned to keep still about his\r\nadventure and introduce no exciting topic. The Widow Douglas stayed by\r\nto see that he obeyed. At home Tom learned of the Cardiff Hill event;\r\nalso that the \xe2\x80\x9cragged man\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x9d body had eventually been found in the river\r\nnear the ferry-landing; he had been drowned while trying to escape,\r\nperhaps.\r\n\r\nAbout a fortnight after Tom\xe2\x80\x99s rescue from the cave, he started off to\r\nvisit Huck, who had grown plenty strong enough, now, to hear exciting\r\ntalk, and Tom had some that would interest him, he thought. Judge\r\nThatcher\xe2\x80\x99s house was on Tom\xe2\x80\x99s way, and he stopped to see Becky. The\r\nJudge and some friends set Tom to talking, and some one asked him\r\nironically if he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to go to the cave again. Tom said he\r\nthought he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind it. The Judge said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, there are others just like you, Tom, I\xe2\x80\x99ve not the least doubt.\r\nBut we have taken care of that. Nobody will get lost in that cave any\r\nmore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I had its big door sheathed with boiler iron two weeks ago, and\r\ntriple-locked--and I\xe2\x80\x99ve got the keys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom turned as white as a sheet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, boy! Here, run, somebody! Fetch a glass of water!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe water was brought and thrown into Tom\xe2\x80\x99s face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, now you\xe2\x80\x99re all right. What was the matter with you, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Judge, Injun Joe\xe2\x80\x99s in the cave!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXIII\r\n\r\nWITHIN a few minutes the news had spread, and a dozen skiff-loads of\r\nmen were on their way to McDougal\xe2\x80\x99s cave, and the ferryboat, well filled\r\nwith passengers, soon followed. Tom Sawyer was in the skiff that bore\r\nJudge Thatcher.\r\n\r\nWhen the cave door was unlocked, a sorrowful sight presented itself in\r\nthe dim twilight of the place. Injun Joe lay stretched upon the ground,\r\ndead, with his face close to the crack of the door, as if his longing\r\neyes had been fixed, to the latest moment, upon the light and the cheer\r\nof the free world outside. Tom was touched, for he knew by his own\r\nexperience how this wretch had suffered. His pity was moved, but\r\nnevertheless he felt an abounding sense of relief and security, now,\r\nwhich revealed to him in a degree which he had not fully appreciated\r\nbefore how vast a weight of dread had been lying upon him since the day\r\nhe lifted his voice against this bloody-minded outcast.\r\n\r\nInjun Joe\xe2\x80\x99s bowie-knife lay close by, its blade broken in two. The great\r\nfoundation-beam of the door had been chipped and hacked through, with\r\ntedious labor; useless labor, too, it was, for the native rock formed a\r\nsill outside it, and upon that stubborn material the knife had wrought\r\nno effect; the only damage done was to the knife itself. But if there\r\nhad been no stony obstruction there the labor would have been useless\r\nstill, for if the beam had been wholly cut away Injun Joe could not have\r\nsqueezed his body under the door, and he knew it. So he had only hacked\r\nthat place in order to be doing something--in order to pass the weary\r\ntime--in order to employ his tortured faculties. Ordinarily one could\r\nfind half a dozen bits of candle stuck around in the crevices of this\r\nvestibule, left there by tourists; but there were none now. The prisoner\r\nhad searched them out and eaten them. He had also contrived to catch a\r\nfew bats, and these, also, he had eaten, leaving only their claws. The\r\npoor unfortunate had starved to death. In one place, near at hand, a\r\nstalagmite had been slowly growing up from the ground for ages, builded\r\nby the water-drip from a stalactite overhead. The captive had broken off\r\nthe stalagmite, and upon the stump had placed a stone, wherein he had\r\nscooped a shallow hollow to catch the precious drop that fell once\r\nin every three minutes with the dreary regularity of a clock-tick--a\r\ndessertspoonful once in four and twenty hours. That drop was falling\r\nwhen the Pyramids were new; when Troy fell; when the foundations of Rome\r\nwere laid; when Christ was crucified; when the Conqueror created the\r\nBritish empire; when Columbus sailed; when the massacre at Lexington was\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cnews.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt is falling now; it will still be falling when all these things shall\r\nhave sunk down the afternoon of history, and the twilight of tradition,\r\nand been swallowed up in the thick night of oblivion. Has everything a\r\npurpose and a mission? Did this drop fall patiently during five thousand\r\nyears to be ready for this flitting human insect\xe2\x80\x99s need? and has it\r\nanother important object to accomplish ten thousand years to come? No\r\nmatter. It is many and many a year since the hapless half-breed scooped\r\nout the stone to catch the priceless drops, but to this day the tourist\r\nstares longest at that pathetic stone and that slow-dropping water when\r\nhe comes to see the wonders of McDougal\xe2\x80\x99s cave. Injun Joe\xe2\x80\x99s cup stands\r\nfirst in the list of the cavern\xe2\x80\x99s marvels; even \xe2\x80\x9cAladdin\xe2\x80\x99s Palace\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n cannot rival it.\r\n\r\nInjun Joe was buried near the mouth of the cave; and people flocked\r\nthere in boats and wagons from the towns and from all the farms and\r\nhamlets for seven miles around; they brought their children, and\r\nall sorts of provisions, and confessed that they had had almost as\r\nsatisfactory a time at the funeral as they could have had at the\r\nhanging.\r\n\r\nThis funeral stopped the further growth of one thing--the petition to the\r\ngovernor for Injun Joe\xe2\x80\x99s pardon. The petition had been largely signed;\r\nmany tearful and eloquent meetings had been held, and a committee of\r\nsappy women been appointed to go in deep mourning and wail around the\r\ngovernor, and implore him to be a merciful ass and trample his duty\r\nunder foot. Injun Joe was believed to have killed five citizens of the\r\nvillage, but what of that? If he had been Satan himself there would\r\nhave been plenty of weaklings ready to scribble their names to a\r\npardon-petition, and drip a tear on it from their permanently impaired\r\nand leaky water-works.\r\n\r\nThe morning after the funeral Tom took Huck to a private place to have\r\nan important talk. Huck had learned all about Tom\xe2\x80\x99s adventure from the\r\nWelshman and the Widow Douglas, by this time, but Tom said he reckoned\r\nthere was one thing they had not told him; that thing was what he wanted\r\nto talk about now. Huck\xe2\x80\x99s face saddened. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what it is. You got into No. 2 and never found anything but\r\nwhiskey. Nobody told me it was you; but I just knowed it must \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 ben\r\nyou, soon as I heard \xe2\x80\x99bout that whiskey business; and I knowed you\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t got the money becuz you\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 got at me some way or other and\r\ntold me even if you was mum to everybody else. Tom, something\xe2\x80\x99s always\r\ntold me we\xe2\x80\x99d never get holt of that swag.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Huck, I never told on that tavern-keeper. _You_ know his tavern\r\nwas all right the Saturday I went to the picnic. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you remember you\r\nwas to watch there that night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes! Why, it seems \xe2\x80\x99bout a year ago. It was that very night that I\r\nfollered Injun Joe to the widder\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_You_ followed him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes--but you keep mum. I reckon Injun Joe\xe2\x80\x99s left friends behind him, and\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t want \xe2\x80\x99em souring on me and doing me mean tricks. If it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nben for me he\xe2\x80\x99d be down in Texas now, all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Huck told his entire adventure in confidence to Tom, who had only\r\nheard of the Welshman\xe2\x80\x99s part of it before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Huck, presently, coming back to the main question, \xe2\x80\x9cwhoever\r\nnipped the whiskey in No. 2, nipped the money, too, I reckon--anyways\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s a goner for us, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck, that money wasn\xe2\x80\x99t ever in No. 2!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat!\xe2\x80\x9d Huck searched his comrade\xe2\x80\x99s face keenly. \xe2\x80\x9cTom, have you got on\r\nthe track of that money again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck, it\xe2\x80\x99s in the cave!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck\xe2\x80\x99s eyes blazed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay it again, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe money\xe2\x80\x99s in the cave!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom--honest injun, now--is it fun, or earnest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEarnest, Huck--just as earnest as ever I was in my life. Will you go in\r\nthere with me and help get it out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bet I will! I will if it\xe2\x80\x99s where we can blaze our way to it and not\r\nget lost.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck, we can do that without the least little bit of trouble in the\r\nworld.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood as wheat! What makes you think the money\xe2\x80\x99s--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck, you just wait till we get in there. If we don\xe2\x80\x99t find it I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nagree to give you my drum and every thing I\xe2\x80\x99ve got in the world. I will,\r\nby jings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right--it\xe2\x80\x99s a whiz. When do you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRight now, if you say it. Are you strong enough?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it far in the cave? I ben on my pins a little, three or four days,\r\nnow, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t walk more\xe2\x80\x99n a mile, Tom--least I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I could.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s about five mile into there the way anybody but me would go, Huck,\r\nbut there\xe2\x80\x99s a mighty short cut that they don\xe2\x80\x99t anybody but me know\r\nabout. Huck, I\xe2\x80\x99ll take you right to it in a skiff. I\xe2\x80\x99ll float the skiff\r\ndown there, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll pull it back again all by myself. You needn\xe2\x80\x99t ever\r\nturn your hand over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLess start right off, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. We want some bread and meat, and our pipes, and a little\r\nbag or two, and two or three kite-strings, and some of these new-fangled\r\nthings they call lucifer matches. I tell you, many\xe2\x80\x99s the time I wished I\r\nhad some when I was in there before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA trifle after noon the boys borrowed a small skiff from a citizen who\r\nwas absent, and got under way at once. When they were several miles\r\nbelow \xe2\x80\x9cCave Hollow,\xe2\x80\x9d Tom said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you see this bluff here looks all alike all the way down from the\r\ncave hollow--no houses, no wood-yards, bushes all alike. But do you see\r\nthat white place up yonder where there\xe2\x80\x99s been a landslide? Well, that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\none of my marks. We\xe2\x80\x99ll get ashore, now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey landed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Huck, where we\xe2\x80\x99re a-standing you could touch that hole I got out\r\nof with a fishing-pole. See if you can find it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck searched all the place about, and found nothing. Tom proudly\r\nmarched into a thick clump of sumach bushes and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere you are! Look at it, Huck; it\xe2\x80\x99s the snuggest hole in this country.\r\nYou just keep mum about it. All along I\xe2\x80\x99ve been wanting to be a robber,\r\nbut I knew I\xe2\x80\x99d got to have a thing like this, and where to run across\r\nit was the bother. We\xe2\x80\x99ve got it now, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll keep it quiet, only we\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nlet Joe Harper and Ben Rogers in--because of course there\xe2\x80\x99s got to be a\r\nGang, or else there wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be any style about it. Tom Sawyer\xe2\x80\x99s Gang--it\r\nsounds splendid, don\xe2\x80\x99t it, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it just does, Tom. And who\xe2\x80\x99ll we rob?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, most anybody. Waylay people--that\xe2\x80\x99s mostly the way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd kill them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not always. Hive them in the cave till they raise a ransom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s a ransom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMoney. You make them raise all they can, off\xe2\x80\x99n their friends; and after\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve kept them a year, if it ain\xe2\x80\x99t raised then you kill them. That\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe general way. Only you don\xe2\x80\x99t kill the women. You shut up the women,\r\nbut you don\xe2\x80\x99t kill them. They\xe2\x80\x99re always beautiful and rich, and awfully\r\nscared. You take their watches and things, but you always take your hat\r\noff and talk polite. They ain\xe2\x80\x99t anybody as polite as robbers--you\xe2\x80\x99ll see\r\nthat in any book. Well, the women get to loving you, and after they\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbeen in the cave a week or two weeks they stop crying and after that\r\nyou couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get them to leave. If you drove them out they\xe2\x80\x99d turn right\r\naround and come back. It\xe2\x80\x99s so in all the books.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s real bully, Tom. I believe it\xe2\x80\x99s better\xe2\x80\x99n to be a pirate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s better in some ways, because it\xe2\x80\x99s close to home and circuses\r\nand all that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy this time everything was ready and the boys entered the hole, Tom in\r\nthe lead. They toiled their way to the farther end of the tunnel, then\r\nmade their spliced kite-strings fast and moved on. A few steps brought\r\nthem to the spring, and Tom felt a shudder quiver all through him.\r\nHe showed Huck the fragment of candle-wick perched on a lump of clay\r\nagainst the wall, and described how he and Becky had watched the flame\r\nstruggle and expire.\r\n\r\nThe boys began to quiet down to whispers, now, for the stillness and\r\ngloom of the place oppressed their spirits. They went on, and presently\r\nentered and followed Tom\xe2\x80\x99s other corridor until they reached the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cjumping-off place.\xe2\x80\x9d The candles revealed the fact that it was not\r\nreally a precipice, but only a steep clay hill twenty or thirty feet\r\nhigh. Tom whispered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you something, Huck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe held his candle aloft and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook as far around the corner as you can. Do you see that? There--on the\r\nbig rock over yonder--done with candle-smoke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, it\xe2\x80\x99s a _cross_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Now_ where\xe2\x80\x99s your Number Two? \xe2\x80\x98_under the cross_,\xe2\x80\x99 hey? Right yonder\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhere I saw Injun Joe poke up his candle, Huck!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck stared at the mystic sign awhile, and then said with a shaky voice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, less git out of here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! and leave the treasure?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes--leave it. Injun Joe\xe2\x80\x99s ghost is round about there, certain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo it ain\xe2\x80\x99t, Huck, no it ain\xe2\x80\x99t. It would ha\xe2\x80\x99nt the place where he\r\ndied--away out at the mouth of the cave--five mile from here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Tom, it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. It would hang round the money. I know the ways of\r\nghosts, and so do you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom began to fear that Huck was right. Mis-givings gathered in his mind.\r\nBut presently an idea occurred to him--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLookyhere, Huck, what fools we\xe2\x80\x99re making of ourselves! Injun Joe\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nghost ain\xe2\x80\x99t a going to come around where there\xe2\x80\x99s a cross!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe point was well taken. It had its effect.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think of that. But that\xe2\x80\x99s so. It\xe2\x80\x99s luck for us, that\r\ncross is. I reckon we\xe2\x80\x99ll climb down there and have a hunt for that box.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom went first, cutting rude steps in the clay hill as he descended.\r\nHuck followed. Four avenues opened out of the small cavern which the\r\ngreat rock stood in. The boys examined three of them with no result.\r\nThey found a small recess in the one nearest the base of the rock, with\r\na pallet of blankets spread down in it; also an old suspender, some\r\nbacon rind, and the well-gnawed bones of two or three fowls. But there\r\nwas no moneybox. The lads searched and researched this place, but in\r\nvain. Tom said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe said _under_ the cross. Well, this comes nearest to being under the\r\ncross. It can\xe2\x80\x99t be under the rock itself, because that sets solid on the\r\nground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey searched everywhere once more, and then sat down discouraged. Huck\r\ncould suggest nothing. By-and-by Tom said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLookyhere, Huck, there\xe2\x80\x99s footprints and some candle-grease on the clay\r\nabout one side of this rock, but not on the other sides. Now, what\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthat for? I bet you the money _is_ under the rock. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to dig in\r\nthe clay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat ain\xe2\x80\x99t no bad notion, Tom!\xe2\x80\x9d said Huck with animation.\r\n\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9creal Barlow\xe2\x80\x9d was out at once, and he had not dug four inches\r\nbefore he struck wood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHey, Huck!--you hear that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck began to dig and scratch now. Some boards were soon uncovered and\r\nremoved. They had concealed a natural chasm which led under the rock.\r\nTom got into this and held his candle as far under the rock as he\r\ncould, but said he could not see to the end of the rift. He proposed\r\nto explore. He stooped and passed under; the narrow way descended\r\ngradually. He followed its winding course, first to the right, then to\r\nthe left, Huck at his heels. Tom turned a short curve, by-and-by, and\r\nexclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy goodness, Huck, lookyhere!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was the treasure-box, sure enough, occupying a snug little cavern,\r\nalong with an empty powder-keg, a couple of guns in leather cases, two\r\nor three pairs of old moccasins, a leather belt, and some other rubbish\r\nwell soaked with the water-drip.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGot it at last!\xe2\x80\x9d said Huck, ploughing among the tarnished coins with\r\nhis hand. \xe2\x80\x9cMy, but we\xe2\x80\x99re rich, Tom!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck, I always reckoned we\xe2\x80\x99d get it. It\xe2\x80\x99s just too good to believe, but\r\nwe _have_ got it, sure! Say--let\xe2\x80\x99s not fool around here. Let\xe2\x80\x99s snake it\r\nout. Lemme see if I can lift the box.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt weighed about fifty pounds. Tom could lift it, after an awkward\r\nfashion, but could not carry it conveniently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought so,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9c_They_ carried it like it was heavy, that day\r\nat the ha\xe2\x80\x99nted house. I noticed that. I reckon I was right to think of\r\nfetching the little bags along.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe money was soon in the bags and the boys took it up to the cross\r\nrock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow less fetch the guns and things,\xe2\x80\x9d said Huck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Huck--leave them there. They\xe2\x80\x99re just the tricks to have when we\r\ngo to robbing. We\xe2\x80\x99ll keep them there all the time, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll hold our\r\norgies there, too. It\xe2\x80\x99s an awful snug place for orgies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat orgies?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dono. But robbers always have orgies, and of course we\xe2\x80\x99ve got to\r\nhave them, too. Come along, Huck, we\xe2\x80\x99ve been in here a long time. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngetting late, I reckon. I\xe2\x80\x99m hungry, too. We\xe2\x80\x99ll eat and smoke when we get\r\nto the skiff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey presently emerged into the clump of sumach bushes, looked warily\r\nout, found the coast clear, and were soon lunching and smoking in the\r\nskiff. As the sun dipped toward the horizon they pushed out and got\r\nunder way. Tom skimmed up the shore through the long twilight, chatting\r\ncheerily with Huck, and landed shortly after dark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Huck,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99ll hide the money in the loft of the widow\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwoodshed, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll come up in the morning and we\xe2\x80\x99ll count it and divide,\r\nand then we\xe2\x80\x99ll hunt up a place out in the woods for it where it will be\r\nsafe. Just you lay quiet here and watch the stuff till I run and hook\r\nBenny Taylor\xe2\x80\x99s little wagon; I won\xe2\x80\x99t be gone a minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe disappeared, and presently returned with the wagon, put the two small\r\nsacks into it, threw some old rags on top of them, and started off,\r\ndragging his cargo behind him. When the boys reached the Welshman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse, they stopped to rest. Just as they were about to move on, the\r\nWelshman stepped out and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHallo, who\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck and Tom Sawyer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood! Come along with me, boys, you are keeping everybody waiting.\r\nHere--hurry up, trot ahead--I\xe2\x80\x99ll haul the wagon for you. Why, it\xe2\x80\x99s not as\r\nlight as it might be. Got bricks in it?--or old metal?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld metal,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI judged so; the boys in this town will take more trouble and fool away\r\nmore time hunting up six bits\xe2\x80\x99 worth of old iron to sell to the foundry\r\nthan they would to make twice the money at regular work. But that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhuman nature--hurry along, hurry along!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boys wanted to know what the hurry was about.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind; you\xe2\x80\x99ll see, when we get to the Widow Douglas\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck said with some apprehension--for he was long used to being falsely\r\naccused:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Jones, we haven\xe2\x80\x99t been doing nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Welshman laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know, Huck, my boy. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know about that. Ain\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nand the widow good friends?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Well, she\xe2\x80\x99s ben good friends to me, anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, then. What do you want to be afraid for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis question was not entirely answered in Huck\xe2\x80\x99s slow mind before he\r\nfound himself pushed, along with Tom, into Mrs. Douglas\xe2\x80\x99 drawing-room.\r\nMr. Jones left the wagon near the door and followed.\r\n\r\nThe place was grandly lighted, and everybody that was of any consequence\r\nin the village was there. The Thatchers were there, the Harpers, the\r\nRogerses, Aunt Polly, Sid, Mary, the minister, the editor, and a great\r\nmany more, and all dressed in their best. The widow received the boys\r\nas heartily as any one could well receive two such looking beings. They\r\nwere covered with clay and candle-grease. Aunt Polly blushed crimson\r\nwith humiliation, and frowned and shook her head at Tom. Nobody suffered\r\nhalf as much as the two boys did, however. Mr. Jones said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom wasn\xe2\x80\x99t at home, yet, so I gave him up; but I stumbled on him and\r\nHuck right at my door, and so I just brought them along in a hurry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you did just right,\xe2\x80\x9d said the widow. \xe2\x80\x9cCome with me, boys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe took them to a bedchamber and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow wash and dress yourselves. Here are two new suits of\r\nclothes--shirts, socks, everything complete. They\xe2\x80\x99re Huck\xe2\x80\x99s--no, no\r\nthanks, Huck--Mr. Jones bought one and I the other. But they\xe2\x80\x99ll fit both\r\nof you. Get into them. We\xe2\x80\x99ll wait--come down when you are slicked up\r\nenough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen she left.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXIV\r\n\r\nHUCK said: \xe2\x80\x9cTom, we can slope, if we can find a rope. The window ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhigh from the ground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShucks! what do you want to slope for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I ain\xe2\x80\x99t used to that kind of a crowd. I can\xe2\x80\x99t stand it. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngoing down there, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, bother! It ain\xe2\x80\x99t anything. I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind it a bit. I\xe2\x80\x99ll take care of\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSid appeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cauntie has been waiting for you all the afternoon. Mary\r\ngot your Sunday clothes ready, and everybody\xe2\x80\x99s been fretting about you.\r\nSay--ain\xe2\x80\x99t this grease and clay, on your clothes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Mr. Siddy, you jist \xe2\x80\x99tend to your own business. What\xe2\x80\x99s all this\r\nblowout about, anyway?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s one of the widow\xe2\x80\x99s parties that she\xe2\x80\x99s always having. This time\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s for the Welshman and his sons, on account of that scrape they\r\nhelped her out of the other night. And say--I can tell you something, if\r\nyou want to know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, old Mr. Jones is going to try to spring something on the people\r\nhere tonight, but I overheard him tell auntie today about it, as a\r\nsecret, but I reckon it\xe2\x80\x99s not much of a secret now. Everybody knows--the\r\nwidow, too, for all she tries to let on she don\xe2\x80\x99t. Mr. Jones was bound\r\nHuck should be here--couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get along with his grand secret without\r\nHuck, you know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSecret about what, Sid?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout Huck tracking the robbers to the widow\xe2\x80\x99s. I reckon Mr. Jones was\r\ngoing to make a grand time over his surprise, but I bet you it will drop\r\npretty flat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSid chuckled in a very contented and satisfied way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSid, was it you that told?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, never mind who it was. _Somebody_ told--that\xe2\x80\x99s enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSid, there\xe2\x80\x99s only one person in this town mean enough to do that, and\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s you. If you had been in Huck\xe2\x80\x99s place you\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 sneaked down the\r\nhill and never told anybody on the robbers. You can\xe2\x80\x99t do any but mean\r\nthings, and you can\xe2\x80\x99t bear to see anybody praised for doing good ones.\r\nThere--no thanks, as the widow says\xe2\x80\x9d--and Tom cuffed Sid\xe2\x80\x99s ears and helped\r\nhim to the door with several kicks. \xe2\x80\x9cNow go and tell auntie if you\r\ndare--and tomorrow you\xe2\x80\x99ll catch it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSome minutes later the widow\xe2\x80\x99s guests were at the supper-table, and a\r\ndozen children were propped up at little side-tables in the same room,\r\nafter the fashion of that country and that day. At the proper time Mr.\r\nJones made his little speech, in which he thanked the widow for the\r\nhonor she was doing himself and his sons, but said that there was\r\nanother person whose modesty--\r\n\r\nAnd so forth and so on. He sprung his secret about Huck\xe2\x80\x99s share in\r\nthe adventure in the finest dramatic manner he was master of, but the\r\nsurprise it occasioned was largely counterfeit and not as clamorous and\r\neffusive as it might have been under happier circumstances. However,\r\nthe widow made a pretty fair show of astonishment, and heaped so many\r\ncompliments and so much gratitude upon Huck that he almost forgot\r\nthe nearly intolerable discomfort of his new clothes in the entirely\r\nintolerable discomfort of being set up as a target for everybody\xe2\x80\x99s gaze\r\nand everybody\xe2\x80\x99s laudations.\r\n\r\nThe widow said she meant to give Huck a home under her roof and have him\r\neducated; and that when she could spare the money she would start him in\r\nbusiness in a modest way. Tom\xe2\x80\x99s chance was come. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck don\xe2\x80\x99t need it. Huck\xe2\x80\x99s rich.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNothing but a heavy strain upon the good manners of the company kept\r\nback the due and proper complimentary laugh at this pleasant joke. But\r\nthe silence was a little awkward. Tom broke it:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck\xe2\x80\x99s got money. Maybe you don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it, but he\xe2\x80\x99s got lots of it.\r\nOh, you needn\xe2\x80\x99t smile--I reckon I can show you. You just wait a minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom ran out of doors. The company looked at each other with a perplexed\r\ninterest--and inquiringly at Huck, who was tongue-tied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSid, what ails Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d said Aunt Polly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe--well, there ain\xe2\x80\x99t ever any\r\nmaking of that boy out. I never--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom entered, struggling with the weight of his sacks, and Aunt Polly\r\ndid not finish her sentence. Tom poured the mass of yellow coin upon the\r\ntable and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere--what did I tell you? Half of it\xe2\x80\x99s Huck\xe2\x80\x99s and half of it\xe2\x80\x99s mine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe spectacle took the general breath away. All gazed, nobody spoke for\r\na moment. Then there was a unanimous call for an explanation. Tom said\r\nhe could furnish it, and he did. The tale was long, but brimful of\r\ninterest. There was scarcely an interruption from any one to break the\r\ncharm of its flow. When he had finished, Mr. Jones said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought I had fixed up a little surprise for this occasion, but it\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t amount to anything now. This one makes it sing mighty small, I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nwilling to allow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe money was counted. The sum amounted to a little over twelve thousand\r\ndollars. It was more than any one present had ever seen at one time\r\nbefore, though several persons were there who were worth considerably\r\nmore than that in property.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXV\r\n\r\nTHE reader may rest satisfied that Tom\xe2\x80\x99s and Huck\xe2\x80\x99s windfall made a\r\nmighty stir in the poor little village of St. Petersburg. So vast a\r\nsum, all in actual cash, seemed next to incredible. It was talked\r\nabout, gloated over, glorified, until the reason of many of the citizens\r\ntottered under the strain of the unhealthy excitement. Every \xe2\x80\x9chaunted\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n house in St. Petersburg and the neighboring villages was dissected,\r\nplank by plank, and its foundations dug up and ransacked for hidden\r\ntreasure--and not by boys, but men--pretty grave, unromantic men, too,\r\nsome of them. Wherever Tom and Huck appeared they were courted, admired,\r\nstared at. The boys were not able to remember that their remarks had\r\npossessed weight before; but now their sayings were treasured and\r\nrepeated; everything they did seemed somehow to be regarded as\r\nremarkable; they had evidently lost the power of doing and saying\r\ncommonplace things; moreover, their past history was raked up and\r\ndiscovered to bear marks of conspicuous originality. The village paper\r\npublished biographical sketches of the boys.\r\n\r\nThe Widow Douglas put Huck\xe2\x80\x99s money out at six per cent., and Judge\r\nThatcher did the same with Tom\xe2\x80\x99s at Aunt Polly\xe2\x80\x99s request. Each lad had\r\nan income, now, that was simply prodigious--a dollar for every weekday in\r\nthe year and half of the Sundays. It was just what the minister got--no,\r\nit was what he was promised--he generally couldn\xe2\x80\x99t collect it. A dollar\r\nand a quarter a week would board, lodge, and school a boy in those old\r\nsimple days--and clothe him and wash him, too, for that matter.\r\n\r\nJudge Thatcher had conceived a great opinion of Tom. He said that no\r\ncommonplace boy would ever have got his daughter out of the cave. When\r\nBecky told her father, in strict confidence, how Tom had taken her\r\nwhipping at school, the Judge was visibly moved; and when she pleaded\r\ngrace for the mighty lie which Tom had told in order to shift that\r\nwhipping from her shoulders to his own, the Judge said with a fine\r\noutburst that it was a noble, a generous, a magnanimous lie--a lie that\r\nwas worthy to hold up its head and march down through history breast to\r\nbreast with George Washington\xe2\x80\x99s lauded Truth about the hatchet! Becky\r\nthought her father had never looked so tall and so superb as when he\r\nwalked the floor and stamped his foot and said that. She went straight\r\noff and told Tom about it.\r\n\r\nJudge Thatcher hoped to see Tom a great lawyer or a great soldier some\r\nday. He said he meant to look to it that Tom should be admitted to the\r\nNational Military Academy and afterward trained in the best law school\r\nin the country, in order that he might be ready for either career or\r\nboth.\r\n\r\nHuck Finn\xe2\x80\x99s wealth and the fact that he was now under the Widow Douglas\xe2\x80\x99\r\nprotection introduced him into society--no, dragged him into it, hurled\r\nhim into it--and his sufferings were almost more than he could bear. The\r\nwidow\xe2\x80\x99s servants kept him clean and neat, combed and brushed, and they\r\nbedded him nightly in unsympathetic sheets that had not one little spot\r\nor stain which he could press to his heart and know for a friend. He had\r\nto eat with a knife and fork; he had to use napkin, cup, and plate;\r\nhe had to learn his book, he had to go to church; he had to talk so\r\nproperly that speech was become insipid in his mouth; whithersoever he\r\nturned, the bars and shackles of civilization shut him in and bound him\r\nhand and foot.\r\n\r\nHe bravely bore his miseries three weeks, and then one day turned up\r\nmissing. For forty-eight hours the widow hunted for him everywhere in\r\ngreat distress. The public were profoundly concerned; they searched high\r\nand low, they dragged the river for his body. Early the third morning\r\nTom Sawyer wisely went poking among some old empty hogsheads down behind\r\nthe abandoned slaughter-house, and in one of them he found the refugee.\r\nHuck had slept there; he had just breakfasted upon some stolen odds and\r\nends of food, and was lying off, now, in comfort, with his pipe. He was\r\nunkempt, uncombed, and clad in the same old ruin of rags that had made\r\nhim picturesque in the days when he was free and happy. Tom routed him\r\nout, told him the trouble he had been causing, and urged him to go home.\r\nHuck\xe2\x80\x99s face lost its tranquil content, and took a melancholy cast. He\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t talk about it, Tom. I\xe2\x80\x99ve tried it, and it don\xe2\x80\x99t work; it don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwork, Tom. It ain\xe2\x80\x99t for me; I ain\xe2\x80\x99t used to it. The widder\xe2\x80\x99s good to me,\r\nand friendly; but I can\xe2\x80\x99t stand them ways. She makes me get up just\r\nat the same time every morning; she makes me wash, they comb me all\r\nto thunder; she won\xe2\x80\x99t let me sleep in the woodshed; I got to wear them\r\nblamed clothes that just smothers me, Tom; they don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to any air\r\ngit through \xe2\x80\x99em, somehow; and they\xe2\x80\x99re so rotten nice that I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nset down, nor lay down, nor roll around anywher\xe2\x80\x99s; I hain\xe2\x80\x99t slid on a\r\ncellar-door for--well, it \xe2\x80\x99pears to be years; I got to go to church\r\nand sweat and sweat--I hate them ornery sermons! I can\xe2\x80\x99t ketch a fly in\r\nthere, I can\xe2\x80\x99t chaw. I got to wear shoes all Sunday. The widder eats by\r\na bell; she goes to bed by a bell; she gits up by a bell--everything\xe2\x80\x99s so\r\nawful reg\xe2\x80\x99lar a body can\xe2\x80\x99t stand it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, everybody does that way, Huck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, it don\xe2\x80\x99t make no difference. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t everybody, and I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n_stand_ it. It\xe2\x80\x99s awful to be tied up so. And grub comes too easy--I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntake no interest in vittles, that way. I got to ask to go a-fishing;\r\nI got to ask to go in a-swimming--dern\xe2\x80\x99d if I hain\xe2\x80\x99t got to ask to do\r\neverything. Well, I\xe2\x80\x99d got to talk so nice it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t no comfort--I\xe2\x80\x99d got\r\nto go up in the attic and rip out awhile, every day, to git a taste\r\nin my mouth, or I\xe2\x80\x99d a died, Tom. The widder wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let me smoke;\r\nshe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let me yell, she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let me gape, nor stretch, nor\r\nscratch, before folks--\xe2\x80\x9d [Then with a spasm of special irritation and\r\ninjury]--\xe2\x80\x9cAnd dad fetch it, she prayed all the time! I never see such a\r\nwoman! I _had_ to shove, Tom--I just had to. And besides, that school\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngoing to open, and I\xe2\x80\x99d a had to go to it--well, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t stand _that_,\r\nTom. Looky-here, Tom, being rich ain\xe2\x80\x99t what it\xe2\x80\x99s cracked up to be. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\njust worry and worry, and sweat and sweat, and a-wishing you was dead\r\nall the time. Now these clothes suits me, and this bar\xe2\x80\x99l suits me, and\r\nI ain\xe2\x80\x99t ever going to shake \xe2\x80\x99em any more. Tom, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever got into\r\nall this trouble if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 ben for that money; now you just take\r\nmy sheer of it along with your\xe2\x80\x99n, and gimme a ten-center sometimes--not\r\nmany times, becuz I don\xe2\x80\x99t give a dern for a thing \xe2\x80\x99thout it\xe2\x80\x99s tollable\r\nhard to git--and you go and beg off for me with the widder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Huck, you know I can\xe2\x80\x99t do that. \xe2\x80\x99Tain\xe2\x80\x99t fair; and besides if you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ntry this thing just a while longer you\xe2\x80\x99ll come to like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike it! Yes--the way I\xe2\x80\x99d like a hot stove if I was to set on it long\r\nenough. No, Tom, I won\xe2\x80\x99t be rich, and I won\xe2\x80\x99t live in them cussed\r\nsmothery houses. I like the woods, and the river, and hogsheads, and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll stick to \xe2\x80\x99em, too. Blame it all! just as we\xe2\x80\x99d got guns, and a cave,\r\nand all just fixed to rob, here this dern foolishness has got to come up\r\nand spile it all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom saw his opportunity--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLookyhere, Huck, being rich ain\xe2\x80\x99t going to keep me back from turning\r\nrobber.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! Oh, good-licks; are you in real dead-wood earnest, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust as dead earnest as I\xe2\x80\x99m sitting here. But Huck, we can\xe2\x80\x99t let you\r\ninto the gang if you ain\xe2\x80\x99t respectable, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck\xe2\x80\x99s joy was quenched.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t let me in, Tom? Didn\xe2\x80\x99t you let me go for a pirate?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but that\xe2\x80\x99s different. A robber is more high-toned than what a\r\npirate is--as a general thing. In most countries they\xe2\x80\x99re awful high up in\r\nthe nobility--dukes and such.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Tom, hain\xe2\x80\x99t you always ben friendly to me? You wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t shet me\r\nout, would you, Tom? You wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do that, now, _would_ you, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t want to, and I _don\xe2\x80\x99t_ want to--but what would people\r\nsay? Why, they\xe2\x80\x99d say, \xe2\x80\x98Mph! Tom Sawyer\xe2\x80\x99s Gang! pretty low characters in\r\nit!\xe2\x80\x99 They\xe2\x80\x99d mean you, Huck. You wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like that, and I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHuck was silent for some time, engaged in a mental struggle. Finally he\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll go back to the widder for a month and tackle it and see if I\r\ncan come to stand it, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll let me b\xe2\x80\x99long to the gang, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, Huck, it\xe2\x80\x99s a whiz! Come along, old chap, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll ask the\r\nwidow to let up on you a little, Huck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you, Tom--now will you? That\xe2\x80\x99s good. If she\xe2\x80\x99ll let up on some of\r\nthe roughest things, I\xe2\x80\x99ll smoke private and cuss private, and crowd\r\nthrough or bust. When you going to start the gang and turn robbers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, right off. We\xe2\x80\x99ll get the boys together and have the initiation\r\ntonight, maybe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave the which?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave the initiation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s to swear to stand by one another, and never tell the gang\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsecrets, even if you\xe2\x80\x99re chopped all to flinders, and kill anybody and\r\nall his family that hurts one of the gang.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s gay--that\xe2\x80\x99s mighty gay, Tom, I tell you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I bet it is. And all that swearing\xe2\x80\x99s got to be done at midnight,\r\nin the lonesomest, awfulest place you can find--a ha\xe2\x80\x99nted house is the\r\nbest, but they\xe2\x80\x99re all ripped up now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, midnight\xe2\x80\x99s good, anyway, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, so it is. And you\xe2\x80\x99ve got to swear on a coffin, and sign it with\r\nblood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, that\xe2\x80\x99s something _like_! Why, it\xe2\x80\x99s a million times bullier than\r\npirating. I\xe2\x80\x99ll stick to the widder till I rot, Tom; and if I git to be\r\na reg\xe2\x80\x99lar ripper of a robber, and everybody talking \xe2\x80\x99bout it, I reckon\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99ll be proud she snaked me in out of the wet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCONCLUSION\r\n\r\nSO endeth this chronicle. It being strictly a history of a _boy_, it\r\nmust stop here; the story could not go much further without becoming the\r\nhistory of a _man_. When one writes a novel about grown people, he knows\r\nexactly where to stop--that is, with a marriage; but when he writes of\r\njuveniles, he must stop where he best can.\r\n\r\nMost of the characters that perform in this book still live, and are\r\nprosperous and happy. Some day it may seem worth while to take up the\r\nstory of the younger ones again and see what sort of men and women they\r\nturned out to be; therefore it will be wisest not to reveal any of that\r\npart of their lives at present.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE ADVENTURES OF TOM SAWYER ***\r\n\r\n***** This file should be named 74-0.txt or 74-0.zip *****\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\n    https://www.gutenberg.org/7/74/\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
